From badd_karma at comcast.net  Mon May  1 09:39:49 2006
From: badd_karma at comcast.net (Garl Grigsby)
Date: Mon, 01 May 2006 09:39:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] External USB 2.0 hard drives
In-Reply-To: <20060429160036.53627bc7.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <4453EA43.9000804@writemoore.net>
	<20060429160036.53627bc7.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <445639D5.8040908@comcast.net>

John Jason Jordan wrote:
> Turning the question around might be easier: Are there any USB drives
> that *don't* work with Linux? (I've heard of problems with USB 2.0, but
> once the user has USB 2.0 working, I've never heard of any drives that
> wouldn't mount.) If there are any, why won't they work?
>   

Yes there are. Anything based on a Genesys Logic USB-IDE chip will fail. 
I bought several external cases to use for backup based on this chip and 
the transfer would fail after transferring only a few hundred megs. 
Sometimes the failure would result in the drive disappearing, sometimes 
it would just stream errors continuously (hundreds and hundreds of megs 
in a few minutes).  There was a work around but it resulted in such poor 
transfer rates that I bit the bullet and purchased a case with a 
different chipset (sorry, can't remember which one right now...). No 
more problems. I now regularly transfer 50+ GB of data with out error.

I've only used two 'prebuilt' usb drives: 1 Seagate and one Western 
Digital. Both work fine. No errors. No problems. I use another

Garl


From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Mon May  1 10:47:21 2006
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Mon, 1 May 2006 10:47:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ADSL dslam...
In-Reply-To: <1146292626.44530993026ee@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1146292626.44530993026ee@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <ca5165530605011047k3c360b06j1bcac748156d1b33@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/28/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> I have a situation where there is a desire for telephone and network
> connectivity in two buildings 200 feet apart.  I have a
> wired/wireless ethernet between the buildings.
>
> One possibility is doing a phoneline and running DSL on top of it.
> Question is, does anyone offer an ADSL dslam that is cheap for a
> single point to point link?  I don't need ADSL2, ADSL2+, SDSL, 24
> ports, etc.
>
> Is there an inexpensive way to feed a POTS signal through an IP network
> and convert it back to POTS at a remote location?  I go from
> 100baseTX to 802.11b for my ip link.  I hear that 100baseTX lines
> have a free pair that can carry POTS. If I can feed POTS over
> 802.11b wifi, I don't need to do DSL.
>
> I have a Verizon modem and a netopia modem from CenturyTel that are
> both ADSL capable.  What is the best way to cash in old DSL modems
> that have no use?  Are there any local businesses that will take them?
>
>

You are right you have several options.  If you already have Ethernet
connectivity
between the two locations just break-out pair #4 from the cable, connect your
phone line to it and connect your phone at the other end.  200ft is
well within the spec
distance for 100base t of 100 meters.  You would not need DSL to do this unless
you want to slow your network speed down or wanted to experiment by running
making your network and phone work on the same pair. If you want to do that
I would recommend the following link:
http://www.pbs.org/cringely/pulpit/pulpit20010823.html

If you decide that you don't want to do DSL then sell them on eBay or
re-cycle them at freegeek.


From charlie at schluting.com  Mon May  1 11:19:40 2006
From: charlie at schluting.com (Charlie Schluting)
Date: Mon, 01 May 2006 11:19:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ADSL dslam...
In-Reply-To: <ca5165530605011047k3c360b06j1bcac748156d1b33@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1146292626.44530993026ee@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<ca5165530605011047k3c360b06j1bcac748156d1b33@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4456513C.8080402@schluting.com>

Larry Brigman wrote:
> On 4/28/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>> I have a situation where there is a desire for telephone and network
>> connectivity in two buildings 200 feet apart.  I have a
>> wired/wireless ethernet between the buildings.
>>
>> One possibility is doing a phoneline and running DSL on top of it.
>> Question is, does anyone offer an ADSL dslam that is cheap for a
>> single point to point link?  I don't need ADSL2, ADSL2+, SDSL, 24
>> ports, etc.
>>

You'd be better off with Long-range ethernet, if you're buying gear. But
200 ft is fine for ethernet, 100bT only requires 4 wires.

Also, you can put two cisco 678's on both sides if you really want. (no
DSMLAM needed)
Assuming the phone line isn't going through the phone company.. because
if it is, that won't work, unless you can get a "raw" circuit (normally
used for security systems, and costs $$$). If you have raw wiring
between the two, just use ethernet.

-Charlie

                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday May 4, 2006
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************
          
                                 PRESENTATION

                                VectorSection
                     (previously known as: UberConverter)
       Open Source File Format Translation for CAD and Vector Graphics

                                      by

                                 Eric Wilhelm
                         <scratchcomputing at gmail.com>
 
      VectorSection uses concepts such as "small pieces, loosely joined" 
      to inform the design of a set of transparent and simple-yet-capable
      interconnected tools for manipulating and translating 2D and 3D 
      vector graphics data between multiple common and uncommon file 
      formats.

      Learning how this system is put together has applications not 
      just to vector graphics, but to data translation/migration in 
      general.

      This presentation will cover the basics of CAD/vector graphics
      interoperability issues and details about how VectorSection allows 
      for ad-hoc extension in any programming or scripting language.
      
      Note:  I have been told that CNN is in town doing a story on 
      Open Source Software in Portland.  Maybe they will come to our
      meeting.  Better yet, they should go to the Lucky Lab after the
      meeting.

Subject: [PLUG] Virtual Interface Limit?
In-Reply-To: <5B0B1CC91321DE4F947C03D9238B267E021B78@cccmail2.chemeketa.network>
References: <5B0B1CC91321DE4F947C03D9238B267E021B78@cccmail2.chemeketa.network>
Message-ID: <4456B3E8.50603@gmail.com>

Hmm...


cnt=0
for a in `seq 1 255`; do
   for b in `seq 1 255`; do
      for c in `seq 1 255`; do
         for d in `seq 1 255`; do
            let cnt=$cnt+1
            ifconfig eth0:$cnt $a.$b.$c.$d;
         done
      done
   done
done


I'm not seeing a limit so far, so I think you'll be ok.  ;-)
~M

>From dev_alloc_name():

const int max_netdevices = 8*PAGE_SIZE;

PAGE_SIZE is 4096.  I think you're probably fine with 32768 virtual interfaces.

From nixlists at writemoore.net  Tue May  2 19:15:54 2006
From: nixlists at writemoore.net (Michael M.)
Date: Tue, 02 May 2006 19:15:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Thunderbird behavior
In-Reply-To: <4457D2E9.5040709@comcast.net>
References: <4457D2E9.5040709@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4458125A.1090105@writemoore.net>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> With t-bird version 1.0.6 (20050716), when I start the program it 
> opens with the inbox displayed. With the new version (1.5.0.2) it 
> opens with the top level display for the e-mail user unless I check 
> the box labeled, "Check for new messages at startup". On one of my 
> machines I never want to read mail. I just installed t-bird for the 
> convenience of sending mail from that machine. On another machine I 
> never want to automatically download mail, since I want to prescreen 
> it with the Comcast web-mail service.
>
> Do any of you t-bird users know some other place from which startup 
> behavior is controlled and/or what you call this "feature"?
>
> Thanks.
>
There's a preference pane item (Edit=>Preferences) under "General" 
called "Thunderbird Start Page" (which, IMO, is an odd thing to call it, 
just 'cause I don't think of an email program as having "pages" in the 
same way, say, a web browser does -- but whatever).  I have never 
changed that setting from the default, but that should be where you can 
control what Tbird shows on start-up.  The default on my install is:

chrome://messenger/content/start.xhtml

As for connecting and downloading mail, that's done on a per account 
basis, so you need to go to Edit=>Account Settings and adjust the server 
settings from there.  It's the same setting you mention -- "Check for 
new messages at startup."

-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue May  2 22:46:07 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 2 May 2006 22:46:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] First Tech Credit Union "microsoftens" web interface
Message-ID: <20060503054607.GB3993@gate.kl-ic.com>


Any other First Tech Credit Union customers here?  They shut down
from April 28 through March 1, to bring up a new web interface.  It
came up today (Tuesday), and was very slow.  It seems to be Internet
Explorer specific, and less capable than the old web interface.  It
did not receive much independent testing.

I am told the annual meeting is coming up soon.  If the interface is
still busted at that time, I plan on making a stink.  Anyone else
want to join in?

Here is an email correspondence I had with their support staff.

----------------------------------------------------------------------

On Tue, May 02, 2006 at 05:34:56PM -0700, HBSupport wrote:
> Thank you for writing. Please attempt to access your account again. You
> should not experience the slowness that you did earlier today. Regarding
> prior history, we are aware of this issue using a browser other than
> Internet Explorer and are working to resolve this. For now, you can view
> prior history using the Internet Explorer browser.

I do appreciate the speedup, thank you.  However, if the prior history
features are not properly enabled in the near future, I will be forced
to move my accounts elsewhere, after 30 years as a member.

Internet Explorer does not run under Linux, nor is it a secure browser
on other platforms.  By requiring this browser, you are subjecting your
customers to a high risk of fraud, and exposing First Tech to enormous
legal liability.  Nationally, Internet Explorer has an 85% share. 
However, Oregon is the open source capital of the world and the Oregon
State University Open Source Labs is the main distribution site for
Mozilla and Firefox, which are growing at more than 40% CAGR.  First
Tech in particular caters to high-tech workers, which have a higher
percentage of Macintosh and Linux users.  I would guess your server
statistics show closer to 30% penetration for alternate browsers,
and growing at a fast clip.

Will alternate browsers be supported by the time of the board meeting,
or will it be necessary to inform the board of the delays and risks,
so they can budget for indemnification and plan appropriate staff
downsizing to accomodate this unfortunate reduction in customer
access?

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

On Wed, 3 May 2006, Eli Stair wrote:

> I have to say if I had the scenario you're seeing I'd make the stink and
> switch financial institutions. Nothing speaks louder than losing customers
> (as long as you let them know), IMO.

/eli,

   Don't get your hopes up too high on this point. Several years ago, Wells
Fargo purchased First Interstate Bank. They closed all the small agricultural
loan branches throughout the western US and forced farmers who had dealt with
their local bankers for decades to call Minneapolis (or some other central
office) for planting loans. They lost 1/3 of all their accounts. It took them
four years to realize that they had lost customers, and started a campaign to
woo them back. Too late. Farmers and growers like to deal with people they
know, and who know them, on a face-to-face basis.

   As companies grow larger they loose touch with the people who keep them in
business.

Rich

Before we get too far down this path, I should point out that First
Tech is a member-operated cooperative, and that the board mostly 
comprises volunteer members elected by the other members.  This has
good and bad features - they are good hearted folk, but they are not
all business professionals.  They will be relying on the professional
managers hired by the organization to provide advice, so my "stink"
should be designed with that in mind.

I've found the best way to reach a volunteer board is with a volunteer
spirit;  it is a new day and I'm a little more level-headed than I was
last night.  If we can get more of the members of the credit union on
board, we can come up with some non-threatening positive solutions.
We should engage the local Perl and Ruby-on-Rails communities for
alternatives to Visual Studio and .NET , for example.

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Wed May  3 08:48:25 2006
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 03 May 2006 08:48:25 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] how to force a reboot with no working rootfs?
Message-ID: <86fyjr3y9i.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


I am working on a problem with embedded hardware, trying to remotely
flash a device.  It involves rudely dd'ing a new filesystem onto a
running linux system.  When dd finishes, the libraries in memory end
up incompatible with the newly flashed filesystem binaries (mounted
read-only).  All I need is a way to reboot so that the system can
reset itself and regain sanity.  I have tried copying the old
/sbin/shutdown to a ramdisk and running that after dd'ing, and that
almost works.  the /tmp/shutdown runs, but something else fails
invisibly because it doesn't shutdown.  I only have network access to
the device.  The solution needs to be easier than driving over (after
arranging access to the power cord) and power cycling.  I am currently
bench testing the procedure on an accessible device.

Any ideas?

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Wed May  3 08:57:19 2006
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Wed, 3 May 2006 08:57:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] First Tech Credit Union "microsoftens" web interface
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0605030811330.30048@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <20060503054607.GB3993@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0605030626450.30048@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<20060503135104.GB11068@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<1146667455.7891.1.camel@localhost.localdomain>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0605030811330.30048@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0605030857q5e8e8003rb6a169913c6c6a6d@mail.gmail.com>

No idea about the specifics of this case, but the motivator is common
across the board:  Money.

The reason this is the result varies, but is usually some combination
of outsourcing the work to other companies, and reducing staff, in
order to reduce costs.  That part gets handled like so many other
cost-cutting moves, goes to a non-highest bidder (not always the
lowest, but usually...).  Companies that are able to reduce their OWN
costs and get the bid can get there  a number of ways, one of which is
their own IT corners.  Many seem to standardize on non-guru coders and
going with "development packages" that pushed by big vendors... that
lock in not only the programmers, but _their_ customers.

/eli

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Wed May  3 09:14:06 2006
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Wed, 3 May 2006 09:14:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] how to force a reboot with no working rootfs?
In-Reply-To: <86fyjr3y9i.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86fyjr3y9i.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0605030914y20f67768h3df807b91371dbb5@mail.gmail.com>

If you have /proc mounted, you can enable a reboot-on-hang bit...
don't remember offhand which entry in sys... there are ways to also
manipulate an immediate reboot with some simple C code without all the
libs necessary for shutdown (check busybox sources).  Alternately, I
recall reading in the docs about embedding magic sysreq functions
(which should allow you to force sync/unmount and reboot).

My last resort usually works if things are really bad:  cat /dev/zero
> /proc/kcore

/eli

On 03 May 2006 08:48:25 -0700, Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> wrote:
>
> I am working on a problem with embedded hardware, trying to remotely
> flash a device.  It involves rudely dd'ing a new filesystem onto a
> running linux system.  When dd finishes, the libraries in memory end
> up incompatible with the newly flashed filesystem binaries (mounted
> read-only).  All I need is a way to reboot so that the system can
> reset itself and regain sanity.  I have tried copying the old
> /sbin/shutdown to a ramdisk and running that after dd'ing, and that
> almost works.  the /tmp/shutdown runs, but something else fails
> invisibly because it doesn't shutdown.  I only have network access to
> the device.  The solution needs to be easier than driving over (after
> arranging access to the power cord) and power cycling.  I am currently
> bench testing the procedure on an accessible device.
>
> Any ideas?
>
>
> --

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Wed May  3 09:38:59 2006
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Wed, 3 May 2006 09:38:59 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] how to force a reboot with no working rootfs?
In-Reply-To: <f4468cae0605030914y20f67768h3df807b91371dbb5@mail.gmail.com>
References: <86fyjr3y9i.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<f4468cae0605030914y20f67768h3df807b91371dbb5@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840605030938r3fa39cdv399036c983a522c5@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/3/06, Eli Stair <eli.stair at gmail.com> wrote:
> If you have /proc mounted, you can enable a reboot-on-hang bit...
> don't remember offhand which entry in sys... there are ways to also
> manipulate an immediate reboot with some simple C code without all the
> libs necessary for shutdown (check busybox sources).  Alternately, I
> recall reading in the docs about embedding magic sysreq functions
> (which should allow you to force sync/unmount and reboot).

I'd go with the sysrq route, read sysrq.txt from the kernel
Documentation directory.  You should be able to enable the sysrq stuff
in /proc/sys/kernel/sysrq (assuming it was turned on in your kernel
build) and trigger lots of interesting things, including the reboot,
by writing the values from the file to /proc/sysrq-trigger.

Jason

> /eli,
> 
>    Don't get your hopes up too high on this point. Several years ago, Wells
> Fargo purchased First Interstate Bank. They closed all the small agricultural
> loan branches throughout the western US and forced farmers who had dealt with
> their local bankers for decades to call Minneapolis (or some other central
> office) for planting loans. They lost 1/3 of all their accounts. It took them
> four years to realize that they had lost customers, and started a campaign to
> woo them back. Too late. Farmers and growers like to deal with people they
> know, and who know them, on a face-to-face basis.
> 
>    As companies grow larger they loose touch with the people who keep them in
> business.

Actually, as companies grow larger they just loose touch with people.
The human aspect of business is albeit lost in most large companies it
seems.  That's why I try to only deal with the little folks.  ;-)  Btw,
I took a gander at your business, Rich, and I like the concept.  Keep up
the good work.  :-)

As far as the 1st Tech web interface, I use OpenSuSE and I don't seem to
have any issues.  However, this is not to say that there isn't some
problems.  I'm finding that it seems the concept of testing the web
interface against multiple browser types as part of web design is not
being properly adhered too anymore.  There seems to be an epidemic of
such lack of attention to detail in this regards all over lately.  I
find that many sites are broken on one browser yet they work with
others.  It's really too bad, in my opinion.

Subject: [PLUG] ADSL dslam...
In-Reply-To: <4456513C.8080402@schluting.com>
References: <1146292626.44530993026ee@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting Charlie Schluting <charlie at schluting.com>:

> Larry Brigman wrote:
> > On 4/28/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> >> I have a situation where there is a desire for telephone and network
> >> connectivity in two buildings 200 feet apart.  I have a
> >> wired/wireless ethernet between the buildings.
> >>
> >> One possibility is doing a phoneline and running DSL on top of it.
> >> Question is, does anyone offer an ADSL dslam that is cheap for a
> >> single point to point link?  I don't need ADSL2, ADSL2+, SDSL, 24
> >> ports, etc.
> >>
> 
> You'd be better off with Long-range ethernet, if you're buying gear. But
> 200 ft is fine for ethernet, 100bT only requires 4 wires.
> 
> Also, you can put two cisco 678's on both sides if you really want. (no
> DSMLAM needed)
> Assuming the phone line isn't going through the phone company.. because
> if it is, that won't work, unless you can get a "raw" circuit (normally
> used for security systems, and costs $$$). If you have raw wiring
> between the two, just use ethernet.
> 
> -Charlie
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug 

I don't want to run physical wire for telephone when I already have
an ip network.  No dry pair, sorry.  There is no free wire pair 
on the 802.11b segment of my interbuilding link, so I can only get a 
phone signal part way using the 4th pair in the wired segment.

Why does all the information about do it yourself DSL that I can find 
assume telco involvement?  Is an SDSL DSLAM the only inexpensive do it 
yourself DSL option?  The modems I might be able to use are not SDSL
modems.

I would be doing DSL on my own private circuit on private property.  
With DSL on the free pair, I can run more than phone through it with 
the right equipment.  If do it yourself POTS to DSL or do it yourself 
POTS to ethernet is available, getting phone and network connections 
everywhere they are wanted in a collection of buildings becomes much 
easier.

DSL seems hard to do in a private setting, but then POTS to IP network
is more attractive.  For one thing, IP packets can be encrypted.  Another
plus, if POTS signal is converted to IP packets, there is no difficulty
sending these packets over IP based wireless links.  Another possibility 
for POTS to IP is being able to relocate where a call is going to via the
Internet.

I don't want to bury wires next to a well and power poles.  I also don't
want to run exposed phone lines.  Even my buried ethernet segment is 
partially exposed because I had a concrete sidewalk in my way.

Regards,

Michael C. Robinson

From plancashire at ci.portland.or.us  Wed May  3 10:28:19 2006
From: plancashire at ci.portland.or.us (Lancashire, Pete)
Date: Wed, 3 May 2006 10:28:19 -0700 
Subject: [PLUG] ADSL dslam...
Message-ID: <9AFECDC22C4D6942BDFFCBDFCDB27A97074ED82F@cityemail.bit.city>

just to toss in a few brain cells .. sorry if not relevent

carefull on what is called POTS, I've used a POTS->IP->POTS
converter before. I did three things (or three I can remember)
1) simulated the on/off hook signals, with the oldie options
like ground start, loop. 2) Did a analogue 8kbs A/DA. i.e.
voice restricted to either of the two telco standards, and 3)
ring detection and generation, 90V/20CPS.

The key being the 8kbs bandwidth.

For 200' I've used and seen used both RF/Microwave, and Laser,
with both either digital and analogue (long long ago).

-pete "Number Please ...."

I really my prefer my "This is how it works" books but since starting
my new career as a linux system administator I confess I occaisionally
need a "Make it GO NOW!!!" manual.

Anybody know a good source for cheap book cases?

John Purser

On Wed, May 03, 2006 at 08:14:42AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Wed, 3 May 2006, Brian Beattie wrote:
> 
> >I'm a member, I don't have the time and energy to be a board member, but
> >I'd be willing to go to the meeting and make a stink.
> 
> Rather than making a comment to Keith I should have asked a question:
> why do organizations change what works for everyone to a format that
> works for only one class of user?

Typically, they don't know any better.  They just went out and bought or
contracted for a "solution".  Decisionmakers rarely have the tech savvy
to ask about cross-platform compatibility.

For the software developers, cross-platform is just an expense, and it
slows down development by exposing bugs and poor design.  Heck, their
customers are not the bank patrons, but the banks themselves.

> Is there a financial incentive for a business such as a credit union
> to change to a Web server that allows only one browser to access it
> correctly? What might be behind these decisions?

A few common ones: end-of-life for their current product; internal
politics; a perceived cost-savings; a really good salesman; someone 
got their underwear in a bunch over some must-have feature; a manager
wanted to display their ability to "shake things up".

-- 
I tend to think of "solution" as just a pretentious term for "thingy".
Doing that word substitution in my head makes IT marketing literature
somewhat more tolerable.  -- lutchann (LWN)

At Linuxfest Saturday, I was talking with a fellow from
http://www.groundworkopensource.com , who was giving away free
copies of GroundWork, a monitoring application.  I mentioned the
old saw about "give away the razors, sell the razor blades", and he
countered with the new open source equivalent:

"Give away the razors, give away the razor blades, sell bandages to
the folks that cut themselves."

Cute.

Subject: [PLUG] how to force a reboot with no working rootfs?
In-Reply-To: <f4468cae0605030914y20f67768h3df807b91371dbb5@mail.gmail.com>
References: <86fyjr3y9i.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

Message-ID: <868xpj3s1x.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Eli" == Eli Stair <eli.stair at gmail.com> writes:

Eli> If you have /proc mounted, you can enable a reboot-on-hang bit...
Eli> don't remember offhand which entry in sys... 

It is /proc/sys/kernel/panic

You echo a value (number of seconds after panic until reset) in there.

Eli> My last resort usually works if things are really bad: cat
Eli> /dev/zero /proc/kcore

I thought of that but it didn't work for some reason.

  Never... and I do mean *never* underestimate the stupidity of an
ol'school IT manager.  I recently worked for one that tried to hire a
.Net job shop to create a Web GUI for a Linux server app.  That's

server.  It also would have required IE on the client side to support
the ActiveX controls.

all the managers thinking this was a great idea.  I barely managed to
kill the project...

  I suspect the 1st Tech managers had no idea they were purchasing an
IE specific app.  The purchasing manager may not understand anything
about the underlying technology.  It's all magic to the uninformed.

Message-ID: <864q073rno.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Jason" == Jason R Martin <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> writes:

Jason> I'd go with the sysrq route, read sysrq.txt from the kernel
Jason> Documentation directory.  You should be able to enable the
Jason> sysrq stuff in /proc/sys/kernel/sysrq (assuming it was turned
Jason> on in your kernel build) and trigger lots of interesting
Jason> things, including the reboot, by writing the values from the
Jason> file to /proc/sysrq-trigger.

Interesting, but unfortunately /proc/sysrq-trigger isn't present on
the existing image's kernel.  I'd have to build a custom kernel for
the device and install it in limited space.  Probably easier to
arrange access to the power supply.

Anything else would not be "buzzword complient".

Every so often there is a fad in web design that everyone MUST have.  Why? 
Because the ads command you!  Without it your web site is not "cool".

It does not matter how stupid or useless it is, if it is a Buzzword 
complient technology, it has to get installed.

.Nyet and AJAX are two of the current buzzword complient technologies 
being pushed.  (And Microsoft is making it seem like you need .Nyet to get 
AJAX, even though their AJAX component breaks pretty much every web 
standard it touches.)

You have to remember that whatis useful or works has no part in corporate 
web design.  It has to do with what the CIO/CEO/CFO/CYA/EIEIO can brag 
about when he meets with all the other clueless TLAs.

"But what does it DO?" - statement never heard in web design review

Yeah, the kcore bit doesn't always work... though I think I remember
using urandom has slightly better results.. probably all a toss of the
dice anyway.

I don't know if it's appropriate, but if you're going to need to do
this regularly you may want to look into installing a STONITH/power
interrupt device in front... rebuilding a kernel with sysrq would
however be cheaper :)

/eli

On 03 May 2006 11:11:07 -0700, Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> wrote:
> >>>>> "Jason" == Jason R Martin <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> writes:
>
> Jason> I'd go with the sysrq route, read sysrq.txt from the kernel
> Jason> Documentation directory.  You should be able to enable the
> Jason> sysrq stuff in /proc/sys/kernel/sysrq (assuming it was turned
> Jason> on in your kernel build) and trigger lots of interesting
> Jason> things, including the reboot, by writing the values from the
> Jason> file to /proc/sysrq-trigger.
>
> Interesting, but unfortunately /proc/sysrq-trigger isn't present on
> the existing image's kernel.  I'd have to build a custom kernel for
> the device and install it in limited space.  Probably easier to
> arrange access to the power supply.
>

On Wed, 3 May 2006 13:45:10 -0700, "Lancashire, Pete" <plancashire at ci.portland.or.us> wrote:
> well i'm stumped
> 
> i have a script where I want to do a string function on
> the last remaining comamnd line argument
> 
>>foobar abcd
> 
> #!/bin/bash
> 
> #this works ...
> arg=$@           # get the argument
> first=${arg:0:1} # get one character, starting at the first
> echo "first:$first"
> 
> #make it all on one line ... does not work
> first=${@:0:1}   # bad subsitution
> 
> what am I doing wrong ? Tried a zillon different ways
> to get the subsitution correct.

I think it's not allowed to put a reference to an array variable in there like that (the @). Probably the easiest way out is:

foo () {
    shift $(( $# - 1 ))
    echo ${1:0:1}
}

$ foo 11 22 33 44
4

hth

Auke

David Fetter wrote:
> On Wed, 2006-05-03 at 06:33 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   
>> On Wed, 3 May 2006, Eli Stair wrote:
>>
>>     
>>> I have to say if I had the scenario you're seeing I'd make the stink and
>>> switch financial institutions. Nothing speaks louder than losing customers
>>> (as long as you let them know), IMO.
>>>       
>> /eli,
>>
>>    Don't get your hopes up too high on this point. Several years ago, Wells
>> Fargo purchased First Interstate Bank. They closed all the small agricultural
>> loan branches throughout the western US and forced farmers who had dealt with
>> their local bankers for decades to call Minneapolis (or some other central
>> office) for planting loans. They lost 1/3 of all their accounts. It took them
>> four years to realize that they had lost customers, and started a campaign to
>> woo them back. Too late. Farmers and growers like to deal with people they
>> know, and who know them, on a face-to-face basis.
>>
>>    As companies grow larger they loose touch with the people who keep them in
>> business.
>>     
>
> Actually, as companies grow larger they just loose touch with people.
> The human aspect of business is albeit lost in most large companies it
> seems.  That's why I try to only deal with the little folks.  ;-)  Btw,
> I took a gander at your business, Rich, and I like the concept.  Keep up
> the good work.  :-)
>   
Totally off-topic, but illustrative of the above nonetheless:  A friend 
who used to live in NYC was in town on business on 9/11, staying at the 
Vista in the World Trade Center.  He left his room 45 minutes before the 
first plane hit.  He stayed that night with another friend, and the next 
day we set out in the late morning to find him some clothes, since the 
only thing he had was the suit he'd been wearing.  (He's a big guy, so 
none of my clothes or those of my friend would fit him.)  As you can 
imagine, New York on 9/12 was a city in shock -- but it was still a 
city.  People live there, people need things, like food.  The local 
businesses, the Mom & Pop hardware store, the neighborhood pharmacy, the 
local markets and clothing stores, even a couple of the community 
bookstores, were open.  The chains and big-box stores were not.  I kept 
thinking, somewhat perversely, of the movie "Miracle on 34th Street," 
and imagining that the Macy's depicted in that movie would have been 
open for business, but the Macy's of today, the one owned by retailing 
giant Federated (which is about to turn Meier & Frank's into a Macy's) 
was not.

It's worth thinking about the next time you're faced with the choice of 
ordering something online or driving 40 miles to a big box store to get 
something that you can buy from a local retailer.  Maybe it would cost a 
little more in the short run, but maybe it would be worth it in the long 
run.

                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday May 4, 2006
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************
          
                                 PRESENTATION

                                VectorSection
                     (previously known as: UberConverter)
       Open Source File Format Translation for CAD and Vector Graphics

                                      by

                                 Eric Wilhelm
                         <scratchcomputing at gmail.com>
 
      VectorSection uses concepts such as "small pieces, loosely joined" 
      to inform the design of a set of transparent and simple-yet-capable
      interconnected tools for manipulating and translating 2D and 3D 
      vector graphics data between multiple common and uncommon file 
      formats.

      Learning how this system is put together has applications not 
      just to vector graphics, but to data translation/migration in 
      general.

      This presentation will cover the basics of CAD/vector graphics
      interoperability issues and details about how VectorSection allows 
      for ad-hoc extension in any programming or scripting language.
      
      Note:  I have been told that CNN is in town doing a story on 
      Open Source Software in Portland.  Maybe they will come to our
      meeting.  Better yet, they should go to the Lucky Lab after the
      meeting.

Subject: [PLUG] how to force a reboot with no working rootfs?
In-Reply-To: <864q073rno.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86fyjr3y9i.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<f4468cae0605030914y20f67768h3df807b91371dbb5@mail.gmail.com>
	<c29537840605030938r3fa39cdv399036c983a522c5@mail.gmail.com>
	<864q073rno.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840605032156v3cb3cbb6x3a2191c99a849d53@mail.gmail.com>

On 03 May 2006 11:11:07 -0700, Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> wrote:
> >>>>> "Jason" == Jason R Martin <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> writes:
>
> Jason> I'd go with the sysrq route, read sysrq.txt from the kernel
> Jason> Documentation directory.  You should be able to enable the
> Jason> sysrq stuff in /proc/sys/kernel/sysrq (assuming it was turned
> Jason> on in your kernel build) and trigger lots of interesting
> Jason> things, including the reboot, by writing the values from the
> Jason> file to /proc/sysrq-trigger.
>
> Interesting, but unfortunately /proc/sysrq-trigger isn't present on
> the existing image's kernel.  I'd have to build a custom kernel for
> the device and install it in limited space.  Probably easier to
> arrange access to the power supply.

Maybe you need a statically-linked version of
shutdown/halt/reboot/whatever?  Or you could write your own little
thing to call the syscall directly.  Or build a static-linked version
of busybox with just the shutdown/halt/reboot/whatever turned on.

Or if you're able to build out-of-tree kernel modules for that kernel,
I seem to recall the RedHat remote crash dump server source comes with
a test kernel module that just causes the system to panic as soon as
it is loaded.  Combined with the automatic reboot on panic option that
should work.  Not recommended for production environments.

Random ideas.  I spend too much time in front of computers.

Jason "for some reason man pages on my Mac won't tell me anything
about Linux syscalls" Martin

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu May  4 10:17:58 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 4 May 2006 10:17:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] First Tech Credit Union "microsoftens" web interface (2)
In-Reply-To: <20060504123520.GD14271@patch.com>
References: <20060503054607.GB3993@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<445985E2.2070703@dslnorthwest.net>
	<20060504074051.GA19851@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<20060504123520.GD14271@patch.com>
Message-ID: <20060504171758.GA27212@gate.kl-ic.com>

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> The error I was referring to was clipped out of my message by a 
> previous poster, hence the confusing message.  I was unable
> to access account history to find out if a check cleared.  They
> "fixed" the problem of no access for non-IE browsers by turning
> off the feature for IE as well, some time in the last 24 hours. 
> "Interesting" approach.  
 
On Thu, May 04, 2006 at 05:35:20AM -0700, Michael Rasmussen wrote:
> Keith, perhaps they're actually doing the right thing. Removing a broken
> element of the interface until they can fix it.   If you were them what would
> you be doing?

0) Don't start what I can't finish
1) See what others have successfully implemented (measure user satisfaction)
2) Learning about interaction design before designing an interface
3) Learning about the composition and expectations of my clientele
4) Generating a set of rank prioritized goals
5) Test dummy mockups of the interface on users
6) Develop a structure for modular addition of features
7) Develop a system for multiplatform, high bandwidth testing
8) Code modules and test the hell out of them
9) Assemble an alpha release and test within a firewall
10) Roll out a beta to a cross section of customers, leaving the old intact
10) Roll out the release, leaving the old operating in parallel
11) After users have had a chance to use both, iterate the design to gain
    user share.
12) When the new design has settled, publish a schedule to remove the old
13) Remove the old design
14) Start a new development process

Any or all of the above steps may be performed by outside companies or
be parts of a closed proprietary process.  However, all must be verifiable
by the organization with fiduciary responsibility, which requires some
degree of openess.

Design without awareness of goals is unprofessional.

Replacing a working system with poorly-tested new code is unprofessional.

Parallel operation of old and new is part of the verification process.
If customers don't use the new interface, it is broken and should be
fixed or abandoned.  Arrogant imposition of a new interface without
customer buy-in is evil.

Look at books about user interaction design by Alan Cooper and others.
Doing this right requires skills that the average coder is not expected
to have, and for a coder to attempt this without the skills is as
unprofessional as a cardiologist attempting brain surgery.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Last night at the Lucky Lab, Eric Wilhelm helpfully suggested
Math::Matrix for a little linear algebra problem I have.  Works great,
but I have one remaining problem.  I want to concatenate the "solution
vector" to another array so that I can print/manipulate it.  I seem to
be doing something ignorant and the solution vector is getting added
to the array as an object.  E.g.:

  $a = new Math::Matrix ([ 1, 0, 0 ]
                         [ 0, 1, 0 ]
                         [ 0, 0, 1 ]);
  $x = new Math::Matrix ([ 1, 2, 3 ]);
  $E = $a->concat($x->transpose);
  $s = $E->solve;

  @output = ($subject,$s->transpose);
  print OUTPUT join(",", at output);

is resulting (annoyingly), as something like:

  001, 1.0  2.0  3.0

but:

  a) I want commas, dammit!

and

  b) I actually want to reformat the values a bit too.

The manual for Math::Matrix doesn't say how to convert from the
supernifty Matrix object to a plain jane array that I want.

I never had this problem in Awk.

I need a giant box labelled "*2000 LBS OF CLUE*" dropped on my head.

From dave at dhdo.org  Fri May  5 11:49:36 2006
From: dave at dhdo.org (Dave)
Date: Fri, 5 May 2006 11:49:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] perl question, Math::Matrix stuff
In-Reply-To: <86d5es1hot.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86d5es1hot.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <200605051149.36308.dave@dhdo.org>

Here's what I found after a bit of digging:
- Math Matrix is bad perl mojo, as it doesn't let you get at the innards well 
if you want to.
- It stores each row and column in an array, internally, which you can access.  
Example code:

use Math::Matrix;
my $a = new Math::Matrix( [1, 2, 3], [4, 5, 6], [7, 8, 9] );

foreach my $col ( 0..2 ) { 
	foreach my $row ( 0..2 ) { 
		print ${$a->[$col]}[$row];
	}
}

Output: 
123456789

You can probably get away with not using hardcoded numbers in the above 
for(each) loops by using the $#array bit (last index of the array). 

Overall, I rate this: "dirty hack"

Subject: [PLUG] perl question, Math::Matrix stuff
In-Reply-To: <200605051149.36308.dave@dhdo.org>
References: <86d5es1hot.fsf@coulee.tdb.com> <200605051149.36308.dave@dhdo.org>

>>>>> "Dave" == Dave  <dave at dhdo.org> writes:

Dave> foreach my $col ( 0..2 ) { foreach my $row ( 0..2 ) { print
Dave> ${$a->[$col]}[$row]; } }

>   $a = new Math::Matrix ([ 1, 0, 0 ]
>                          [ 0, 1, 0 ]
>                          [ 0, 0, 1 ]);
>   $x = new Math::Matrix ([ 1, 2, 3 ]);
>   $E = $a->concat($x->transpose);
>   $s = $E->solve;
>
>   @output = ($subject,$s->transpose);
>   print OUTPUT join(",", at output);

Changing the @output assignment line to:

  @output = ($subject,@{$s->transpose->[0]);

does what I want.  While perhaps not a 2000 lb block, the provided
clue was altogether sufficient.  Thanks!

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Fri May  5 14:23:00 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Fri, 5 May 2006 14:23:00 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] VMware Guest OS Hangup
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0605051413270.32397@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   When I upgraded my VMware to 5.0 (and now 5.5), I lost the ability to share
files between the host linux network and the guest win98se2. I've been on the
phone with the VMware support tech for more than an hour and the guest 0S can
ping any of the linux hosts, and vice versa. But, the guest OS cannot mount
the shared drive.

   I believe this is because I moved the linux mount point from an nfs-mounted
filesystem (/mnt/usr2/winstuff) to a local filesystem (/data2/winstuff), and
win98 can no longer find it because it has the former path wired in.
/etc/samba/smb.conf includes:

; share name -- modified by RBS 2004-09-22
[winstuff]
    comment = Shared directory for group vmwin98
    path = /data2/winstuff
    writable = yes
    valid users  = @vmwin98
    locking = yes
    create mode = 0660
    directory mode = 0770

   However, when I try to 'map network drive' from within the virtual win98's
drive e:\, the default path '\\salmo\winstuff' returns 'not found.' I think
there must be a way to define the new path properly, but I don't know what
that is. When I've tried to specify the path as '\\salmo\data2\winstuff' I'm
told that's not valid because it could not be found.

   I'd like someone to tell me where in win98 I can change the binding of
\\salmo\winstuff to the new mount point. Or, how to define that within the
map network drive dialog box. Please. I've been fighting this for a long time
and I need to share data between the last two win apps and my linux system.

TIA,

Rich

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Fri May  5 21:16:24 2006
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Fri, 5 May 2006 21:16:24 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] perl question, Math::Matrix stuff
In-Reply-To: <200605051149.36308.dave@dhdo.org>
References: <86d5es1hot.fsf@coulee.tdb.com> <200605051149.36308.dave@dhdo.org>
Message-ID: <200605052116.25116.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Dave
# on Friday 05 May 2006 11:49 am:

>Here's what I found after a bit of digging:
>- Math Matrix is bad perl mojo, as it doesn't let you get at the
> innards well if you want to.

use overload
       '~'  => 'transpose',
       '+'  => 'add',
       '-'  => 'subtract',
       '*'  => 'multiply',
       '""' => 'as_string';

>Overall, I rate this: "dirty hack"

Not really.  It's actually pretty well written OO.  Whether overloading 
in Perl is a dirty hack, well -- in an "everything is a string" 
language, it sometimes gets confusing.

The first time you run into it, it might seem weird.  If it's not done 
well, the DWIM might bite you.  But overall it's pretty 
straightforward.

Now, explain why the pure, clean, and consistent OO language has this 
speed-bump in the syntax?

#!/usr/bin/ruby

puts a=lambda {|x|
  f=x+1
  s=f**2
  s/f
}.call(2)

a=lambda do |x|
  f=x+1
  s=f**2
  s/f
end.call(2)
puts a

puts a=lambda do |x|
  f=x+1
  s=f**2
  s/f
end.call(2)

--Eric

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Fri May  5 21:24:16 2006
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Fri, 5 May 2006 21:24:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] perl question, Math::Matrix stuff
In-Reply-To: <86d5es1hot.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86d5es1hot.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <200605052124.16308.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Russell Senior
# on Friday 05 May 2006 10:53 am:

>? @output = ($subject,$s->transpose);
>? print OUTPUT join(",", at output);

Sorry to leave you hanging all day.  Looks like you figured it out.  
Dereference to get a list.

  @output = ($subject,@{$s->transpose->[0]});

Your earlier output contained ($scalar, $object) where $object would 
typically print as something like the following:

  $ perl -e 'print bless({}, "foo");'
  foo=HASH(0x814cc20)

Or actually, this is an array ref under the hood.

  $ perl -e 'print bless([], Math::Matrix);'
  Math::Matrix=ARRAY(0x814cc20)

But, operator overloading on "" is on, thus the ugly dump builtin 
stringification gets turned into $object->as_string().

>I never had this problem in Awk.

Does awk do operator overloading?

Don't worry.  I still don't quite get it either :-)

  $ perl -e 'print scalar(%{{a => b}})'
  1/8

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri May  5 21:57:15 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Fri, 5 May 2006 21:57:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Transfer from thumb drive to Windows computer
Message-ID: <20060505215715.ca5e2ee7.johnxj@comcast.net>

So today I was at PSU and needed to print out a document that I had
created in OO.o Writer. I had my 512 MB thumb drive with me. So first I
exported from Writer as PDF, then used Konqueror to copy the PDF file
to the thumb drive. To do so all I needed to do was stick the thumb
drive in a USB port -- it's blue light came on and Konqueror saw it
immediately. Evidently it just auto-mounts. Before copying the PDF file
to the thumb drive I decided I no longer needed any of the files that
were on it, so I deleted them all. The drive was empty when I copied
the PDF file to it.

Having done that I schlepped to the computer lab at the library. I
logged into one of the university's Windows computers, stuck the thumb
drive in one of its USB ports, and tried to print the PDF file. Big
problem -- no PDF file. The Windows computer saw the thumb drive, but
instead of a PDF file it saw a file called reglog.txt. I opened the
reglog.txt file in Notepad and it appeared to be tags, like field names
for a database. Except mostly the tags were empty, although a few had
data in them. And the data was definitely from my computer -- one
"field" had my first name, another had my hotpop e-mail address.
Definitely the file came from my Ubuntu-64 Breezy computer.

Note: The thumb drive is relatively new. It has never been mounted on
my Windows desktop. It has only been used on the Linux laptop, and then
only once before today.

At that point I took my laptop out, booted it up, put the thumb drive
back in a USB port, and all that appears to be on it is the PDF file.
No file named reglog.txt. The PDF file opens for viewing in Konqueror
and is exactly the document that I created.

I finally got past the problem by using my Linux laptop (sitting on a
chair next to me at the lab) to e-mail the file to my pdx.edu e-mail
account, then logging into the account on the university's computer to
retrieve the file. 

Before this happens to me again, I need to figure out what is going on.
I hope someone here can uninscrute the inscrutable madness that caused
this bizarro situation. Any ideas?

1) Where did the reglog.txt file come from? It must have been created by something on my Linux laptop because the "tags" contain data that could have come only from this computer. Nothing dangerous, just things like my username on my hotpop e-mail account and such that could not possibly have been generated by the Windows computer at the PSU computer lab. What application or utility on my Linux computer created it and put it on the thumb drive? Why? What possible purpose could it have? And why is it invisible to Linux (Konqueror does not show it, and I have Show Hidden Files checked, nor does it show up with slocate.)

2) Why does Windows not see the PDF file on the thumb drive?

As to Brian's question regarding the filesystem on the thumb drive, it must be Fat32. I don't know the Linux command to check it, but I know when it arrived it was new in the packaging, and I never reformatted it. Furthermore, both Windows and Linux seem to be able to read and write to it.

Having said that, things on the Linux laptop bring up a couple questions. Having verified that my Windows desktop treats it the same as the computer at PSU, I unmounted it there and plugged it into the laptop, on which I am writing this with Sylpheed. Sylpheed can navigate to /media/thumbdrive, see the file, and attach it to this e-mail. Konqueror can see it too, and can even display it using Kpdf, which functions as a plugin for Konqueror. However, Adobe Reader 7.0 from chroot cannot see anything in /media except "floppy" and "cdrom," neither of which actually exist (no floppy at all, and the optical drive is cdrom0). But Adobe Reader has lots of other problems on Linux, so perhaps that doesn't prove anything. I also have PDF Viewer installed, and it can open and display the file fine.

More interesting is what happens when I try to find it from the command line. It does not show up at all. (The file is Runes.pdf.) I tried "sudo slocate Runes.pdf" from the root and it found

/home/jjj/.kde/share/apps/kpdf/22627.Runes.pdf.xml
/home/jjj/History_English/Runes.pdf
/chroot/breezy/32bits/home/jjj/.kde/share/apps/kpdf/22627.Runes.pdf.xml
/chroot/breezy/32bits/home/jjj/History_English/Runes.pdf

I am assuming the first is something Konqueror created when I used it to view the document with the Kpdf plugin. The second entry is the original file. And the last two must be links. (I've never understood why everything shows up in chroot as well as in the real world, but that is a lesson for another day.) In sum, slocate doesn't see the file on the thumb drive. shows there is just the root of the drive:

jjj at Devil5:~$ cd /media/thumbdrive
jjj at Devil5:/media/thumbdrive$ ls -al
total 32
drwxrwxrwx  2 jjj jjj  4096 2006-05-05 15:21 .
drwxr-xr-x  8 jjj jjj  4096 2006-03-17 11:35 ..
-rw-rw-rw-  1 jjj jjj 22627 2006-05-05 15:17 Runes.pdf

Did I interpret that correctly?

On the Windows machine it shows no directories either. 

It occurred to me about half an hour ago that maybe instead of copying
the file to the thumbdrive, Linux just made a link to the file which
remains on the laptop's hard drive as the only location where it really
exists. To test this I used Konqueror to drag another copy to the thumb
drive. When I did so Konqueror gave me the options Move here, Copy
here, Make link here, and Cancel. If I told it to Copy it to the thumb
drive, Konqueror immediately bitched that there was already an
identical file on the thumb drive.

But y'all know that GUI stuff lies a lot. All real truth is revealed
from the command line, right? Is there a command line way to tell what
the file on the thumb drive really is? A genuine copy of the file or a
link?

This is so bizarro. There must be an explanation.

> remains on the laptop's hard drive as the only location where it really
> exists. To test this I used Konqueror to drag another copy to the thumb
> drive. When I did so Konqueror gave me the options Move here, Copy

> from the command line, right? Is there a command line way to tell what
> the file on the thumb drive really is? A genuine copy of the file or a
> link?
>

ls -l would show you if it were a link. Instead of the normal file name,
it would be something like foo.pdf -> /home/blah/foo.pdf.

Look through dmesg when you insert the usb drive to see what the device
node for the drive is, it should be something like /dev/sda. To check for
partitions run fdisk -l /dev/sda and it'll tell you what's on it.

From sofar at foo-projects.org  Sat May  6 13:53:16 2006
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)
Date: Sat, 06 May 2006 13:53:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <20060505220455.7465949f.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20060505220455.7465949f.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <445D0CBC.3070909@foo-projects.org>

John Jason Jordan wrote:
> Today while trying to boot my Ubuntu-64 Breezy laptop at PSU (see other
> post a few minutes ago), Ubuntu decided it do a file system check.
> "Booted 30 times, mandatory check" or something like that was the
> message.
> 
> This has happened numerous times in the past. But today (naturally) it
> happened when I was in a hurry and anxious to get going (a lab
> assistant was standing by waiting for my computer to boot so I could
> show him what was really on my thumb drive). It takes almost five
> minutes for it to go through the file system check.
> 
> I don't mind that it wants to check the filesystem, it's just that it
> always decides to do it at the most inconvenient times. I need a "Not
> Now!" command. I tried Ctrl-c but that just allows me to restart the
> computer. When it restarts it still wants to check the filesystem.
> Anyone know how to get past the automatic filesystem check? Better yet,
> is there a way to change it so it stops and asks me before it
> automatically does the check?

1) man fsck ;^)
2) it's distro dependent, some allow you to touch /fastboot and get it over 
with quickly, check your mount init.d script
3) you can change the check interval with e2fstune and turn it off that way 
too (just make sure you actually *do* fsck it once in a while ;^)

Cheers,

Auke

Windows 2000 desktop. There is a copy in c:\documents and settings\john

permanently on the Windows 2000 desktop that I use for backups.

Clearly I need it to be FAT32, but the man page didn't give the syntax for that parameter. Anyone know a more complete tutorial on formatting commands?

> I looked at man mkfs it listed tons of options, of which I understood only
> one -- unless you specify a filesystem it will format it ext2. Clearly I
> need it to be FAT32, but the man page didn't give the syntax for that
> parameter. Anyone know a more complete tutorial on formatting commands?

   Disk /dev/sda: 255 heads, 63 sectors, 30401 cylinders
   Units = cylinders of 16065 * 512 bytes

      Device Boot    Start       End    Blocks   Id  System
   /dev/sda1             1      5200  41768968+  83  Linux
   /dev/sda2         30264     30394   1052257+  83  Linux
   /dev/sda3         30395     30401     56227+  82  Linux swap
   /dev/sda4          5201     30263 201318547+   f  Win95 Ext'd (LBA)

   /dev/sda5          5201     30263 201318516    b  Win95 FAT32
<http://www.nslu2-linux.org/wiki/HowTo/FAT32?action=edit>?

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sun May  7 11:39:35 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sun, 7 May 2006 11:39:35 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Man pages rock! (OK, sometimes, maybe)
Message-ID: <20060507113935.82f58f0c.johnxj@comcast.net>

OMFG a man page actually had an answer I needed and solved a problem.
This is a FIRST!

This morning I finally figured out what was wrong with my thumb drive.
It was never mounted on the Linux computer. Hence, when I copied files
to it, I was really just copying them to the mount point, which is
really just a folder. That also explains why Linux couldn't see
reglog.txt that the Windows desktop put on it. Pretty dumb, eh? OK, I
never claimed to be smart.

So, after considerable trial and error I finally got it mounted, and I
copied a couple files to it as a test. Then I wanted to move it to the
Windows desktop to see if Windows could actually see the files. But I
couldn't get it unmounted. All I got was "the device is busy."
It's /dev/sdb1 and the commands I used were sudo umount /media
thumbdrive (the mount point) and sudo umount /dev/sdb1. Same error
message either way. I also tried using Krusader, a GUI file manager
that has a mount/unmount utility. It saw it as mounted, but when I
click to unmount it (running Krusader as root) it gave me the same "the
device is busy" error message.

Google wasn't helping so, in desperation I tried man umount. And there
was the answer. I needed a "lazy" unmount. So "sudo unmount
-l /dev/sdb1" did the trick. Then I did "mount" to verify that it was
no longer listed as mounted, unplugged it, and took it to the Windows
computer. Lo and behold, Windows happily saw the files I put on it from
the Linux computer! Yay!

I've added this precious information to my CheatSheet file.

Now, for my next learning experience I'm going to see if I can figure
out how to tell if /media/thumbdrive is the thumbdrive or just the
mount point. Like, dragging files there with Konqueror or anything else
doesn't display any difference. They appear there whether they were
copied just to the folder or to the device, and there is no difference
in the way they appear in the GUI. That was the whole problem --
something should have told me "look dumbass, you're just copying the
files to the mount point, not to the device because you're so stupid
you never mounted the device."

The most common reason (for me) that I get the device busy errors is
because I have a shell window open where the current working directory
is inside the mounted device filesystem.  That is enough for the
system to consider the device in use, hence the messages.  The other
way to have it in use is for a program to be open with one of the
files in the mounted filesystem open, or even to have started the
program from a command shell inside the mounted filesystem (i.e. "cd
/mnt/blah; acroread blah.pdf; cd /; umount /mnt/blah" won't work).

It's strange, but you'll get used to it.  Windows is much happier to
rip the filesystem out from underneath a running program, which causes
interesting things to happen sometimes.

Jason

A trick I used to use was to create in the mount point, i.e., with the
thumb drive NOT mounted, a file named "UNMOUNTED".  if I saw that
file, I knew I hadn't mounted the device.

mounted via 'df' or whatever.

>> you can control what Tbird shows on start-up.  The default on my 
>> install is:
>> chrome://messenger/content/start.xhtml
>
> That's for something different. When I check that box, I get a 
> "Welcome to Mozilla Thunderbird!" web page in the pane that displays 
> the contents of a selected message. It has links to various pages at 
> mozillaZine or the main mozilla page, that it brings up in Firefox.
>
>> As for connecting and downloading mail, that's done on a per account 
>> basis, so you need to go to Edit=>Account Settings and adjust the 
>> server settings from there.  It's the same setting you mention -- 
>> "Check for new messages at startup."
>
> I understand that, and for awhile I had T-bird set up to handle more 
> than one account. But it always used to start up with a list of 
> folders in the left pane, and a list of messages in the top-right 
> pane. Now, it starts up with the list of folders in the left pane, and 
> a single pane on the right with what you get if you click on the 
> account name at the top of the list of folders.
>
> I'm trying to have Inbox selected automatically on startup.
>
Don't know what to tell you, because that's exactly what happens when I 
start up Tbird:  list of folders on the left, with "Inbox" for my 
default account pre-selected; list of messages in the Inbox on the 
right.  I don't use the preview pane (that is, I keep it closed), so for 
me it's just left & right, not left & top right/bottom right.  I have 
only two accounts -- my default is one that connects to my (remote) IMAP 
mail server; the other is just "Local Folders."  Tbird is configured to 
connect to the server at startup and sync.

Maybe double-check and make sure you have designated a default account 
-- i.e, the one whose Inbox you want displayed?  Otherwise, there are 
some startup settings in about:config you might try fiddling with, but 
your guess is as good as mine as to what setting might have the desired 
effect.

In-Reply-To: <050720060246.20107.445D5F7C0006A9B200004E8B2205886442020102020E039D@comcast.net>

rmannon at comcast.net wrote:
> Just stumbled across this.  Really kind of sad in my eyes.  To me, it seems like once again it's not about knowing.  It's all about the money here in the US. 
>
> http://www.theregister.co.uk/2006/05/04/wcit_open_source/
>   

Ok,, it's Sunday and getting late, so maybe my reading comprehension 
skills are taking a break, but can someone explain this?:

"Legal fears also seem on the rise with regard to open source software, 
according to panel moderator and attorney Hank Jones. He has seen major 
acquisitions delayed or cancelled after pursuers put the software assets 
of their target under the microscope. The "increased scrutiny that is 
now occurring" has started to turn up some horror stories, Jones said. 
In particular, companies have discovered that assets claimed as 
proprietary often end up having plenty of code pinched from various open 
source projects."

How is that a problem for open source development?  If the acquisition 
targets are committing fraud (by claiming assets they don't have -- in 
this case, claiming proprietary software assets that are, in fact, 
violating other's copyrights), then that's a problem with these 
companies' proprietary software development methods and models, not with 
open source development.  Wouldn't it seem that this kind of thing 
should be an argument in favor of open source development, in part 
because open source development removes the temptation to claim assets 
one doesn't own?

Fraud is fraud, whatever development model you're talking about.  If a 
company is misrepresenting its assets and open source development makes 
it easier to catch them before investors pump money into the company, I 
would think that's a good thing.

> Now!" command. I tried Ctrl-c but that just allows me to restart the
> computer. When it restarts it still wants to check the filesystem.
> Anyone know how to get past the automatic filesystem check? Better yet,
> is there a way to change it so it stops and asks me before it
> automatically does the check?

tune2fs -i 0 -c 0 /dev/XXX

Well, /assuming/ you have an ext3 filesystem.

Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc> wrote:

> On Mon, 2006-05-08 at 09:41 -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:

> > But where do I put it? The system hasn't booted yet, so I guess I can't
> > go to a command line. This must be entered in some startup script,
> > right? Im too new to Linux to know which one. I read the man page for
> > tune2fs, but it assumed I knew where to use the command.
> 
> You just need to run it once; it updates the filesystem metadata the
> e2fsck uses to determine whether to fsck or not.

I don't think John wanted to permanently turn off disk checks.  He just
wanted to be able to skip them when they were inconvenient.

On my Redhat systems I can pass a 'fastboot' argument on the boot command
line to skip the disk checks.  You could create a separate entry in grub
that passes this argument, giving you the option to boot with disk checks
skipped.  Another option which should work on most systems is to boot
into single user mode, touch /fastboot, then exit.  This would boot the
system with disk checks skipped.

galen

to disable them altogether and (maybe) sometimes force a fsck.  I don't
recall the last time I actually did a fsck in Linux, except when I was
experimenting with things and causing crashes.

From dave at dhdo.org  Mon May  8 12:07:30 2006
From: dave at dhdo.org (Dave)
Date: Mon, 8 May 2006 12:07:30 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <445F8EE4.1050607@telestream.com>

Message-ID: <200605081207.30661.dave@dhdo.org>

On Monday 08 May 2006 11:33, m0gely wrote:
> Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
> I've been watching this too and am puzzled.  What's the point at all?
> If the system is being shutdown properly then fsck shouldn't be needed
> on boot outside of special scenarios.  BSD doesn't do this.

To quote:  

http://batleth.sapienti-sat.org/projects/FAQs/ext3-faq.html

Q: If a system shutdown hard, even with journaling is it at all necessary to 
run e2fsck?

Theodore Ts'o said:
It's best to just always run e2fsck. [...]
E2fsck will run the journal automatically, and if the filesystem is otherwise 
clean, it skip doing a full filesystem check.
If the filesystem is not clean (because during the previous run the kernel 
noticed some filesystem inconsistencies), e2fsck will automatically do a full 
check if it is necessary.
If you have multiple disks, fsck will run multiple e2fsck processes in 
parallel, thus speeding up your boot sequence than if you let the kernel 
replay the journal for each filesystem when it tries to mount it, since then 
the journal replays will be done sequentially, instead of in parallel.



Or, in fewer words, "Yeah."
-- 
-Dave

From alan at clueserver.org  Mon May  8 12:25:41 2006
From: alan at clueserver.org (alan)
Date: Mon, 8 May 2006 12:25:41 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <445F8EE4.1050607@telestream.com>
References: <20060508170314.AABB18F13@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
	<1147111177.30695.13.camel@willow.odshp.com>
	<445F8A38.4020202@verizon.net> <445F8EE4.1050607@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0605081216240.10035@blackbox.fnordora.org>

On Mon, 8 May 2006, m0gely wrote:

> Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
>> I have been watching this thread and wondering why I hadn't seen this 
>> forced fsck happen.  It finally occured to me...I rarely shut my Ubuntu box 
>> off.  It hasn't seen 30 boots since I installed Ubuntu on it.  Judging by 
>> what I am reading here, maybe I should manually cause the system to run 
>> fsck once in a while???
>
> I've been watching this too and am puzzled.  What's the point at all? If the 
> system is being shutdown properly then fsck shouldn't be needed on boot 
> outside of special scenarios.  BSD doesn't do this.

There are two times that it will try to force an fsck.

1) When the partition is marked "unclean".  This is when the partition was 
not properly unmounted before a reboot.

2) When the partition has reached a maximal count. This is a parameter 
that is set by tune2fs.  If you are running a machine that has frequent 
reboots, this will happen more frequently than you would want.

It is the second situation that is causing the forced checks.

--

I mean in general, FreeBSD and OpenBSD in my own use doesn't force an

that compared some file systems:

http://www.debian-administration.org/articles/388

He also posted a link to a discussion of the article at LWN:
http://lwn.net/Articles/181343/

I think you'd probably find both are helpful and informative.

John Jason Jordan wrote:
> Now, for my next learning experience I'm going to see if I can figure

> doesn't display any difference. They appear there whether they were

> in the way they appear in the GUI. That was the whole problem --
> something should have told me "look dumbass, you're just copying the

Is this a problem with the way Ubuntu encourages creating mount points 
under /media instead of under /mnt? I read, I think on the Ubuntu 
mailing list or forum, that you should create mount points under /media 
so that Ubuntu will display them on the desktop when mounted. That would 
be with the default Gnome environment anyway; I'm not sure whether it 
would be different with Kubuntu or Xubuntu. But if you had created your 
mount point under /mnt (say, /mnt/thumbdrive instead of 
/media/thumbdrive), would Konqueror have let you copy files there if the 
drive wasn't actually mounted?

I don't have a USB stick nor do I use Konqueror -- I'm just curious 
whether you'd have had the same issue if you used /mnt. I know I found 
Ubuntu's way of doing things a little confusing because I am used to 
thinking of /mnt as the place for mounting removable media and storage. 
I thought that Linux file system hierarchy standard had decreed it so.

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Mon May  8 23:14:43 2006
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Mon, 8 May 2006 23:14:43 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Man pages rock! (OK, sometimes, maybe)
In-Reply-To: <44602150.6090700@writemoore.net>
References: <20060507113935.82f58f0c.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<44602150.6090700@writemoore.net>
Message-ID: <c29537840605082314r436d347dyb3aa2c95e118e64a@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/8/06, Michael M. <nixlists at writemoore.net> wrote:
> John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > Now, for my next learning experience I'm going to see if I can figure
> > out how to tell if /media/thumbdrive is the thumbdrive or just the
> > mount point. Like, dragging files there with Konqueror or anything else
> > doesn't display any difference. They appear there whether they were
> > copied just to the folder or to the device, and there is no difference
> > in the way they appear in the GUI. That was the whole problem --
> > something should have told me "look dumbass, you're just copying the
> > files to the mount point, not to the device because you're so stupid
> > you never mounted the device."
>
> Is this a problem with the way Ubuntu encourages creating mount points
> under /media instead of under /mnt? I read, I think on the Ubuntu
> mailing list or forum, that you should create mount points under /media
> so that Ubuntu will display them on the desktop when mounted. That would
> be with the default Gnome environment anyway; I'm not sure whether it
> would be different with Kubuntu or Xubuntu. But if you had created your
> mount point under /mnt (say, /mnt/thumbdrive instead of
> /media/thumbdrive), would Konqueror have let you copy files there if the
> drive wasn't actually mounted?
>
> I don't have a USB stick nor do I use Konqueror -- I'm just curious
> whether you'd have had the same issue if you used /mnt. I know I found
> Ubuntu's way of doing things a little confusing because I am used to
> thinking of /mnt as the place for mounting removable media and storage.
> I thought that Linux file system hierarchy standard had decreed it so.

/media is the location for removable media, such as cdrom, usb
thumbdrives, etc. as of FHS-2.3.  Rationale from the spec
(http://www.pathname.com/fhs/):

"Historically there have been a number of other different places used
to mount removeable media such as /cdrom, /mnt or /mnt/cdrom. Placing
the mount points for all removeable media directly in the root
directory would potentially result in a large number of extra
directories in /. Although the use of subdirectories in /mnt as a
mount point has recently been common, it conflicts with a much older
tradition of using /mnt directly as a temporary mount point."

Jason

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Tue May  9 00:58:35 2006
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Tue, 9 May 2006 00:58:35 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] firewall
In-Reply-To: <44604914.7000506@verizon.net>
References: <44604914.7000506@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0605090058pa9c48a8ifde907d4670d13ef@mail.gmail.com>

If you're running the nmap scans from your machine, then you will
likely bypass most (all) typical firewall rules.  A standard allow
rule in most "kit" setups is to allow all connections to the loopback
(localhost / 127.0.0.1) and to the public ethernet if the origin is
the same interface.  You didn't specify, so that's my assumption.

What I'd be most worried about is tha telnet is running at all...
first order of busines is to decide why it is, and move to using SSH
for remote access.   Second, track down an externally-originated scan
of your host to get a view of what your firewall rules are blocking;
the nmap website has at least one link to such a site/service IIRC.

Congrats on taking the interest and effort into looking at system security!

/eli

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Tue May  9 08:52:34 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Tue, 09 May 2006 08:52:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <44601136.3060806@telestream.com>
References: <20060508170314.AABB18F13@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
	<1147111177.30695.13.camel@willow.odshp.com>	<445F8A38.4020202@verizon.net>
	<445F8EE4.1050607@telestream.com> <445FAAE4.7060005@writemoore.net>
	<44601136.3060806@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <1147189954.21024.7.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Mon, 2006-05-08 at 20:49 -0700, m0gely wrote:

> I mean in general, FreeBSD and OpenBSD in my own use doesn't force an 
> fsck on a reboot unless the volume wasn't marked clean such as in the 
> case of a power outage.  To put it another way, and to the best of my 
> knowledge, the BSD's do not force an fsck on reboots just for safety's 
> sake when all is well.

BSD expected you to periodically check your filesystems, but did not
traditionally implement a mechanism to remind, let alone force, you to
do so.  In fact, SMM:1, "Installing and Operating 4.4BSD" recommends
checking file systems EVERY WEEK or so.  (pg 51, sect 6.3 "Filesystem
checks, backups, and disaster recovery")  Now, obviously no one checks
filesystems every week and filesystem stability has improved
considerably in the 13 years since that was written.  But periodic
maintenance has always been expected.

http://www.netbsd.org/Documentation/bsd/lite2/smm.html

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

On Mon, 2006-05-08 at 20:14 -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> On Mon, 8 May 2006 21:48:42 +0000
> Auke Kok <sofar at foo-projects.org> dijo:
> 
> > by default ext3 gets checked every N time *or* every N days, 
> > whichever comes first. Sooner or later you'll hit the 180days 
> > mark which is the default for the N days value ;^)
> 
> Very interesting discussion, which I am finding very educational! A few questions:
> 
> 1) I haven't experienced any problem in the past year running my hard
> drive with ext3. So what would y'all recommend for a laptop that is
> rebooted an average of 1.5 times a day and for which a backup of /home
> + is made every time a significant amount of new stuff is added (like
> a day or two of work)? Like, how often should fsck be run? I'm not
> interested in *absolute* safety; I'm interested in what is reasonable.
> E.g., in the final analysis, we're all dead anyway. So what is a
> reasonable tradeoff between safety and usability?

As I said, I just turn the periodic checks off with ext3.  If you're
paranoid, turn off the mount-count and just keep the 180 day checks.

> 2) I just ordered a new 80 GB 7200 rpm hard disk for my laptop. Should
> I put ext3 on it, or might something else be better? 

ext3 will give you the least headaches.  If you were experienced admin
and had an application that required something the other filesystems
might provide, it might warrant it.  Otherwise, stick with the
mainstream.

Wil

> On Monday 08 May 2006 11:33, m0gely wrote:
> > Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
> > I've been watching this too and am puzzled.  What's the point at all?
> > If the system is being shutdown properly then fsck shouldn't be needed
> > on boot outside of special scenarios.  BSD doesn't do this.
> 
> To quote:  
> 
> http://batleth.sapienti-sat.org/projects/FAQs/ext3-faq.html
> 
> Q: If a system shutdown hard, even with journaling is it at all necessary to 
> run e2fsck?
> 
> Theodore Ts'o said:
> It's best to just always run e2fsck. [...]
> E2fsck will run the journal automatically, and if the filesystem is otherwise 
> clean, it skip doing a full filesystem check.
> If the filesystem is not clean (because during the previous run the kernel 
> noticed some filesystem inconsistencies), e2fsck will automatically do a full 
> check if it is necessary.
> If you have multiple disks, fsck will run multiple e2fsck processes in 
> parallel, thus speeding up your boot sequence than if you let the kernel 
> replay the journal for each filesystem when it tries to mount it, since then 
> the journal replays will be done sequentially, instead of in parallel.
> 
> 
> 
> Or, in fewer words, "Yeah."

Or in a few more words, "yeah, if the system was 'shutdown hard' (i.e.,
did not umount cleanly)."  Otherwise, "nope."

Wil

In the many years that I have used Windows NT-2000 on my desktop I have experienced that only three or four times. But when it happens Windows *always* forces a filesystem check on rebooting. However, it never forces a filesystem check otherwise. It seems to me that this might be the proper strategy for me on my Ubuntu laptop -- turn it off, but make it happen if Linux was shut down dirty. I just need to figure out what utilities I need and how to use them.
