Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Mon, 2006-05-08 at 20:49 -0700, m0gely wrote:
> 
>> I mean in general, FreeBSD and OpenBSD in my own use doesn't force an 
>> fsck on a reboot unless the volume wasn't marked clean such as in the 
>> case of a power outage.  To put it another way, and to the best of my 
>> knowledge, the BSD's do not force an fsck on reboots just for safety's 
>> sake when all is well.
> 
> BSD expected you to periodically check your filesystems, but did not
> traditionally implement a mechanism to remind, let alone force, you to
> do so.  In fact, SMM:1, "Installing and Operating 4.4BSD" recommends
> checking file systems EVERY WEEK or so. 

Ignoring net/open BSD..
We aren't talking about Linux 2.0, so let's not talk about BSD 4.4 either :)

In FreeBSD 5 and above, if the file system is marked dirty (because of a
power failure or the like) the system does not stop booting. Background
fsck is run instead.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue May  9 11:37:58 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 9 May 2006 11:37:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <20060509095750.2e4ea01c.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <445F8A38.4020202@verizon.net>
	<855602249c4c0b45374c79ed0a692ae9@localhost>
	<20060508201445.2bc264f0.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<1147190405.21024.15.camel@willow.odshp.com>
	<20060509095750.2e4ea01c.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20060509183758.GB4263@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Tue, May 09, 2006 at 09:57:50AM -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
...
> Unfortunately, that happens to me a lot. About once a week I lock up
> the gnome shell and am left with no mouse or keyboard. As far as I
> know Linux is still running just fine underneath, but since I can't
> get to it I have no choice but to hit the power button. 
...

This is often fixable with "Cntrl-Alt-Backspace".  Linux is not
like Windows;  the GUI runs on top of X-windows in user space,
separately from the underlying operating system.  That means that
the X-Windows layer can be stopped and started without rebooting
and without corrupting disks.  Yes, there are some poorly written
Gnome applications that can lock up Gnome (since all are in user
space, all can potentially step on each other), but something must
be hosed indeed for user space activity to take out a running kernel.

You should write down, as best as you can remember, what you were
doing and what applications were running when the crash occured. 
I know you use an external mouse, for example.  A poorly designed
external mouse might "vibrate" between neighboring pixels and
generate a hell of a lot of interrupts, which at some busy times might
overwhelm Linux.  A new mouse might fix that.  It is good to keep a
text window open on one of your virtual desktops, and occasionally
type "free" and "uptime"; you may notice that the memory use or load
average is climbing an hour or two before your crash.

If CT-AL-BS doesn't bring you back (rapidly) to a new login window,
and if you cannot ping the machine from elsewhere when hooked up to
the ethernet,  then your kernel is indeed stopping.  This requires
rebooting, and you should look for the cause.

If the kernel is locking up, my best guess is that the display driver
for your laptop is flawed.  Probably closed-source for your video
chipset, right?  It just goes to show how open-source is more practical
than closed.  Keep an eye out for updated display drivers;  sometimes
even proprietary vendors manage to get a clue and fix things.

If the display driver is not the culprit, another thing to check on
a laptop is built-in features that are enabled but that you are not
using.  For example, you might have a serial port or a modem that
are enabled in the BIOS, but Linux doesn't know about them or how
to handle their interrupts.  A mouse movement might get interpreted
as a modem call, perhaps.  Go into the BIOS during powerup and turn
off the features you don't need.

The point is, a reasonably well tuned Linux system does not need
rebooting.  My laptop gets suspended when I am not using it, but
it stays booted for weeks or months.  Every once in a great while,
I need to reboot it (like this weekend, when it decided it wouldn't
mount a compact flash card), but I have never seen a gnome-caused
lockup that a CT-AL-BS wouldn't fix.  Yes, rebooting can sometimes
be faster than finding the root cause of a problem, but in the long
run it is better to clean up all the problems.  Linux and open source
give you the power to do so.

Going back to the original thread about fsck, perhaps I should be
purposely forcing a full fsck from time to time.  As it is, it only
happens at 7 or 8 month intervals, that is 180 days plus the weeks
until the next reboot.  I would feel more secure if fsck ran more
often than that.  Sometimes high uptime is too much of a good thing!

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

If you do that in initlevel 5 I believe it tries to restart X.

One of the first things I do on a new install is change the default 
runlevel to 3.  Saves me a lot of headaches when I have to rebuild my X 
drivers.  (NVIDIA drivers do not like to be rebuilt while X is running. 
Something about unloading modules in use...)

From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Tue May  9 11:47:23 2006

Message-ID: <20060509184723.GA19106@nellump.ath.cx>

On Tue, May 09, 2006 at 10:35:14AM -0700, Jason R. Martin wrote:

> I curious why telnet is running on your router.

It's not unusual for an embedded system such as a cheap router or
access point to have a telnet server running.  It's low-impact, and
Windows users don't have to install new software to log in (a telnet
client is already there, available through the DOS shell). The telnet
daemon usually only listens on LAN-side interfaces (i.e., a scan
from the Internet won't show anything listening on port 23).

-- 
Paul

On Tue, 09 May 2006 11:34:44 -0700
m0gely <m0gely at telestream.com> dijo:

> Timothy J. Bruce wrote:
> 
> > Can you use <Ctrl><F1> (I think that's the keystroke) to get a CLI login? 
> > If you can / do, you could log in and kill X and force it to restart. 
> 
> Or possibly ctrl+alt+back space while in X?  That's the three finger 
> solute for X and will kill it.

Everone seems to have missed a tidbit in what I said earlier. When it
locks up, the keyboard and mouse are BOTH dead. So Ctrl-this and
Alt-that ain't gonna happen. The only button that still works is the
power button.

It would be nice to figure out what is causing it, but from my
experience it is pretty random, with one possible exception: Kdar will
lock things up about one backup out of three. Normally I don't back up
my mp3s because I keep them in a separate folder outside of /home, plus
I have them all copied to my Windows computer as well, but once when I
did Kdar locked up, and again on the second attempt. There are about 14
GB of them. This leads me to believe it might be a USB problem, since
the backups are going to the pocket drive on USB. Otherwise, the
lockups seem to be random, although, as Keith points out, I do have an
external USB mouse. So if there is something flaky going on with USB,
that could account for the rest of the lockups.

I have always enjoyed the fact that my external USB mouse "just works."
I can boot the computer completely without plugging it in, and when I
do, it instantly works. I can unplug it, plug it back in, and it works.
It's just perfectly hot-swappable. The touchpad has an on-off button
which I normally leave off because I hate it. But if I turn it on the
touchpad immediately starts to work too, whether it was on when I
booted the computer or not. I can have both working at the same time if
I want to, although normally I use just the external mouse. The
touchpad is just for situations where I get to the university and
discover that I left the external mouse sitting at home on the desk.
Because both work so perfectly it's hard to believe that either is the
cause of the lockups.

As for video drivers, originally I used the open source video drivers.
They ran just fine except that I was never able to get a DVD to play.
Then one day several months ago I learned how to install the
proprietary nVidia drivers. That fixed the DVD problem. However, it
locked up before and continues to lock up afterwards, with about the
same frequency, so I doubt the nVidia drivers had anything to do with
it. The nVidia proprietary drivers not only permit playing DVDs, but
the screen refresh is faster, so I want to keep them -- unless, of
course, I eventually discover that they are the source of the problem.

> the screen refresh is faster, so I want to keep them -- unless, of
> course, I eventually discover that they are the source of the problem.

If the keyboard and mouse are completely locked up (CTL-ALT-Backspace
doesn't work to kill X, etc.) then it sounds more like a kernel panic.
 Are your keyboard LEDs blinking (caps lock LED for example)?

Jason

I have always just assumed it was gnome that was locking up, not Linux. But what do I know?

Hi Dave,

I'm with Open Country (http://www.opencountry.com).  We would like to 
have the item below posted to your list if you would be so kind.  We 
believe it isn't SPAM because:

1 .It would be of real interest to some of your members
2. It involves Linux
3. We're posting from legit addresses
4. We're not trying to sell anything
5. We need input from qualified domain experts on generalized issues 
regarding Linux.
6. We won't send any further messages to the list, and we will not place 
anyone on any marketing list because this is an R&D effort.

Thanks!

Have fun!

**************************************
Open Country is looking for IT systems administrators who administer 20
or more Linux/UNIX systems at a single location to participate in a survey.

The first 100 respondents will receive a $5 gift certificate to
Starbucks or Think Geek.  All respondents can opt to receive the survey
results and to have a chance to earn $200 for further research.

The survey is online at http://www.surveymonkey.com/s.asp?u=645642117890

Further research will be done with a sub-group of the online survey
respondents who will be asked to participate in an interview survey.
The interview is done in a focus group fashion over the phone or in
person.  The interview will take no more than an hour.

Interview participants do not have to fill out lengthy forms or
questionnaires and will be paid $200 and receive a copy of the overall
survey results.

Please contact Paul Cubbage at pcubbage at opencountry.com to apply.

Thanks!

From beattie at beattie-home.net  Wed May 10 06:45:37 2006
From: beattie at beattie-home.net (Brian Beattie)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 06:45:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] mixing netmasks
Message-ID: <1147268737.7873.6.camel@localhost.localdomain>

Somebody I know is proposing to mix netmasks on the same physical
network.  That is to say all machines that are supposed to talk to each
other (servers and clients of those servers) would be in 10.1.1.0/24.
Engineering machines (prototypes, machines under test) would be in
10.1.0.0/16 in the 10.1.2 range and engineering workstations, which
needed to talk to the servers and the test macnines would be in the
10.1.1.0 range with a netmask of 255.255.0.0

I told him that that was a bad idea, and I think it would cause
problems.  Am I talking through my hat again? (most of the machines on
the 10.1.1.0/24 net are M$ boxen and most of the engineering
workstations are Linux.

From aaron at madebyai.com  Wed May 10 08:36:16 2006
From: aaron at madebyai.com (Aaron Ten Clay)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 08:36:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] mixing netmasks
In-Reply-To: <1147268737.7873.6.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1147268737.7873.6.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <200605100836.16850.aaron@madebyai.com>

On Wed May 10 2006 06:45, Brian Beattie wrote:
> Somebody I know is proposing to mix netmasks on the same physical
> network.  That is to say all machines that are supposed to talk to each
> other (servers and clients of those servers) would be in 10.1.1.0/24.
> Engineering machines (prototypes, machines under test) would be in
> 10.1.0.0/16 in the 10.1.2 range and engineering workstations, which
> needed to talk to the servers and the test macnines would be in the
> 10.1.1.0 range with a netmask of 255.255.0.0
> 
> I told him that that was a bad idea, and I think it would cause
> problems.  Am I talking through my hat again? (most of the machines on
> the 10.1.1.0/24 net are M$ boxen and most of the engineering
> workstations are Linux.

The only "problem" you'll run into is that any machines as 10.1.1.0/24 (the windows boxen) will NOT be able to talk to machines that are 10.1.anythingbut1.X because their netmask tells them that address is not local and they must use a gateway to reach it. Having a router routeback on the same interface is considered very bad practice and there's absolutely no reason to do it.

As suggested by another listee, routers were invented precisely for this situation. Either give all the machines on the segment the same netmasks, or make two segments.

If the idea is to make two "networks" and prevent the machines from talking, you must have separate physical networks because anyone can change the IP address or spoof ARP packets or any other malicious methods of seeing data you don't want them to. At the very least, use two completely separate ranges (10.1.1.0/24 and 10.1.2.0/24 or the like) instead of overlapping two ranges with different sizes.

-Aaron

From Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu  Wed May 10 08:38:40 2006
From: Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu (Jeff Moore)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 08:38:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RE: mixing netmasks
References: <20060510134607.45B0F45BE@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <5B0B1CC91321DE4F947C03D9238B267E020AEA@cccmail2.chemeketa.network>

Somebody I know is proposing to mix netmasks on the same physical
network.  That is to say all machines that are supposed to talk to each
other (servers and clients of those servers) would be in 10.1.1.0/24.
Engineering machines (prototypes, machines under test) would be in
10.1.0.0/16 in the 10.1.2 range and engineering workstations, which
needed to talk to the servers and the test macnines would be in the
10.1.1.0 range with a netmask of 255.255.0.0

I told him that that was a bad idea, and I think it would cause
problems.  Am I talking through my hat again? (most of the machines on
the 10.1.1.0/24 net are M$ boxen and most of the engineering
workstations are Linux.

It certainly is a bad idea. First thing to consider is that each subnet once designated will have a gateway and a broadcast address at the bottom and top of the range. These will overlap with the larger subnet that you have created. For anything machines outside the larger subnet commuicating with machines in the smaller subnets there will have to be an appropriate router. If there is not a router for each subnet then the broadcast traffic will not be seen by most of the machines and conversely most of the machines will not be able to access the default gateway on the larger subnet. The reason I say most is that the lowest subnet broken out of the larger subnet will be able to access the gateway x.x.x.1(but not the broadcast address) and the last subnet broken out of the larger subnet will be able to access the broadcast address x.x.x.255(but not the gateway).
You may want to encourage him to try it. Its a great excersise. We just had one of our admins assign his new servers to a /24 when the network it was on was a 23. That was interesting. if you established an outbound(offnet) session with the server then you could access it from other nets just fine. But once the arp record staled in the router it would fall off line. The problem was that he was on the fist leg of the larger subnet so he could access the gateway and move traffic but when you tried to access his server from off net the broadcast was going to the x.x.x.255 on a different net.
Fun...
Hope my ramblings help!
Jeff M 

From mikeraz at patch.com  Wed May 10 08:50:13 2006
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 08:50:13 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] mixing netmasks
In-Reply-To: <1147268737.7873.6.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1147268737.7873.6.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <61136.170.135.112.12.1147276213.squirrel@mail.patch.com>


Brian Beattie wrote:
> Somebody I know is proposing to mix netmasks on the same physical
> network.

This sounds like a form of multi-netting which, in and of itself is OK.

> That is to say all machines that are supposed to talk to each
> other (servers and clients of those servers) would be in 10.1.1.0/24.
> Engineering machines (prototypes, machines under test) would be in
> 10.1.0.0/16 in the 10.1.2 range and engineering workstations, which
> needed to talk to the servers and the test macnines would be in the
> 10.1.1.0 range with a netmask of 255.255.0.0

I'm a little confused by your description.  Is this restatement of it correct?

  Hosts           NetRange
Srvrs Clients   10.1.1.0/24
Engineering     10.1.0.0/16 network addresses assigned in 10.1.2.0/24,
prototypes and test boxes
Eng Wkstations  10.1.1.0/16 (nonsensical network/netmask definition)

Do the engineering boxes in 10.1.0.0/16 need to talk with the servers in
10.1.1.0/24?  The issue you'll have, with the current definition, is the
10.1.1.0/24 machines will need a gateway router to return traffic to to 10.1.0.0
or 10.1.2.0 address ranges.

Of course this assumes that the netmasks you've described will be applied to the
hosts.  If the admin is using the netmasks as shorthand to describe the use of the
10.1.0.0/16 address space and not the network configuration on the hosts and in
fact every machine will have a netmask of 255.255.0.0 then it won't be a problem.

   Michael Rasmussen, Portland, Ore, USA
  Be Appropriate && Follow Your Curiosity
        http://www.patch.com/words/

Making the machine fail consistently is a desirable first step.  A good
way to exercise it is to build a kernel.  See if you can find a script
on the net that will repeatedly build a kernel and check the results.
I'm fairly certain that something like this is out there.

If you just want to move forward, the next thing I would try is swapping
power supplies.

From slinkywizard at integraonline.com  Wed May 10 12:35:56 2006
From: slinkywizard at integraonline.com (Max)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 12:35:56 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] mixing netmasks
In-Reply-To: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0605100732090.4068@arrowhead.galois.com>
References: <1147268737.7873.6.camel@localhost.localdomain>
	<Pine.OSX.4.64.0605100732090.4068@arrowhead.galois.com>
Message-ID: <63415.64.122.164.5.1147289756.squirrel@webmail-devel.integraonline.com>

> On Wed, 10 May 2006, Brian Beattie wrote:
>
>> Somebody I know is proposing to mix netmasks on the same physical
>> network.  That is to say all machines that are supposed to talk to each
>> other (servers and clients of those servers) would be in 10.1.1.0/24.
>> Engineering machines (prototypes, machines under test) would be in
>> 10.1.0.0/16 in the 10.1.2 range and engineering workstations, which
>> needed to talk to the servers and the test macnines would be in the
>> 10.1.1.0 range with a netmask of 255.255.0.0


Could you elaboratle on "physical"? Perhaps he's using VLANS (Virtual
LANS) to segment the traffic. Using VLANS is common for such an activity,
as you get the benefit of broadcast isolation, which won't occur if you
are simply multinetting on the same LAN segment.

Regards,
Max

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Wed May 10 12:49:54 2006
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 12:49:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Only on Windoz/AutoCAD
In-Reply-To: <9AFECDC22C4D6942BDFFCBDFCDB27A97074ED897@cityemail.bit.city>
References: <9AFECDC22C4D6942BDFFCBDFCDB27A97074ED897@cityemail.bit.city>
Message-ID: <200605101249.54332.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Lancashire, Pete
# on Wednesday 10 May 2006 11:34 am:

>what advantage would there be to have a FOSS CAD
>package, other then cost ? I would love to be able
>to push FOSS CAD to some of my ME - Architect - Civil E
>- etc friends but have not found what to say while
>on the FOSS Server/Desktop soapbox.

umm.  Hackability?  Vendor-independent interoperability?  Inter-office 
change tracking?  Contract/working-drawings/BOM integration that 
actually works?  Custom software for small businesses pushing the 
envelope?  Any other number of things that Scratch Computing aims to 
enable?

Seriously, the de Young cladding would been practically impossible and 
riddled with human error had it not been for the hardcore automation, 
and that wasn't even feasible to do as a commercial cad system plugin 
((a)  startup cost, lock-in risk, and vendor evaluation latency would 
have basically killed it, (b) the project requirements changed about 
daily (more on that at tonight's perl-mongers if you're so inclined), 
and (c) GUI-based/VB/whatever automation can really suck when all you 
need to do is twiddle an input and regen.)

In the current environment, it is still really difficult to get any sort 
of CAD users onto Linux.  QCad isn't going to do it.  I don't believe 
it is going to grow out of community contribution either -- engineers 
might know how to program FORTRAN, Pascal, etc.  But, even if they've 
got mad C, C++, Perl, Ruby, or Python skills, the fact is that they are 
way too worn out at the end of the day to try to assemble a complex 
software architecture into their head and hammer out some code.  So, 
the Linux/Apache codebase growth model is looking bleak, what about the 
Mozilla/OpenOffice/Blender opening-of-proprietary-code model?  Well, I 
guess AutoDesk could decide to drop that bomb whenever they feel like 
it, but my guess is that there is way too much licensed "not my IP" in 
there to make that feasible for them or anyone else.  The government 
funding to BRLCAD is encouraging, but they are heavily focused on 
simulating ballistics and designing tanks, so usability suffers.

Which brings us to private funding.  I'm not seeing a huge list of open 
source CAD software development companies with $2M seed funding and a 
business model to make it work.  I've been particularly focused on 
trying to find a sustainable-growth model involving custom development 
leveraging open-source, but the combined challenges of "lack of 
infrastructure, lack of time, lack of sales, and trying to get 
something in a price-point that works for small businesses" really 
stack up against it.

Okay, getting closer to on-topic...

How many people on this list know of engineers, architects, etc. that 
would like to be running Linux if it weren't for that sticky CAD issue?  
I would be happy to hear from them on or off list.  I'm certainly up 
for putting together something (maybe even at OSCamp) if there is 
enough interest.

--Eric
-- 
I arise in the morning torn between a desire to improve the world and a
desire to enjoy the world. This makes it hard to plan the day.
--E.B. White

    http://scratchcomputing.com

From sofar at foo-projects.org  Wed May 10 13:52:35 2006
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 20:52:35 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] how to get screenshots of text installs
In-Reply-To: <200605101342.11938.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200605101342.11938.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <b51b6356203ef7b940d139e66a879e1c@localhost>



On Wed, 10 May 2006 13:42:11 -0700, Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> wrote:
> On Wednesday 10 May 2006 11:01 am, Galen Seitz wrote:
>> Scott Chapman <scott_list at mischko.com> wrote:
>> 
>> > Carla Schroder wrote:
>> > > Anyone know how to capture screenshots of text Linux install
>> > > screens, like Debian sarge?
>> > 
>> > Install it in a virtual machine?
>> > 
>> 
>> Serial console and kermit/minicom?
>> 
> 
> I'll give it a try. Don't feel like messing with virtual environments.
> Once I 
> heard that hitting Ctrl+Print Screen saved screenshots to the root folder,
> 
> but it's never worked for me. What good is a Print Screen key anyway,
> hmph. 

while vmware is nice and gives you decent speed, it also costs money

qemu is free, and perhaps slowish (slower than vmware), maybe fast enough for a text installer (for an example installer screenshots in qemu see http://lunar-linux.org/~sofar/1.5.0-screenshots/installer-boot.html ). Toss on the non-free but gratis kqemu and performance is acceptable, especially on faster systems.

bochs is sloooow but would work the same as qemu.

serial console is uber-fast (native cpu speed) but usually requires some tweaking and/or cable mess. You can get the same effect by using qemu with console->serial->terminal redirection, and capture using ^A^H by running it all under 'screen', maybe doesn't work too well with colors though

My preferred method still is qemu since the fonts are acceptable and it's relatively easy to use a grabber to get the window, and all free.

Cheers,

Auke

On Wed, 10 May 2006 18:23:52 -0700 (PDT)
Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> dijo:

>    I have perhaps a dozen letters that I would like to have scanned as .pdf
> files. Kinko's wants too much, IMO, for the service. I know of no one with a
> scanner, and I don't have sufficient need to spend a lot of money on one.
> 
>    Any recommendations on an inexpensive scanner that produces readable text
> (as in a scanned letter) and is known to work with xsane?

I can't remember thde website, but if you google for it there is a website devoted exclusively to scanners that work with Linux. I do have an old scanner (ancient, actually) connected to my Windows 2000 desktop, but I wanted a new one and one that was 1) portable and 2) completely compatible with Linux. I ended up getting a Canon LiDE 30 and it works perfectly with Xsane and the other Linux scanning utilities. I couldn't be happier.

OCR, on the other hand, is an much sadder story. Basically, it hasn't made it to Linux yet. So if you want to scan and OCR, good luck. (I did find a free website that would OCR from a TIFF file, and that is what I have been using, even though it does a pretty crappy job.)

If you can wait until the next clinic, I can bring my scanner with and will be happy to scan your documents for you. In fact, there is a PDXLinux meeting tomorrow (Thursday) at the Fireside that I plan to attend. I could bring it and we could do it there. Let me know, as I would not ordinarily bring it unless I know someone needs it (another 2 Kg in my pack).


From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Wed May 10 20:01:14 2006
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Wed, 10 May 2006 20:01:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Inexpensive Letter-size Scanner For Linux
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0605101821230.32705@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

Message-ID: <20060511030114.GA15360@nellump.ath.cx>

On Wed, May 10, 2006 at 06:23:52PM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
 
>   Any recommendations on an inexpensive scanner that produces readable text
> (as in a scanned letter) and is known to work with xsane?

If you have a SCSI adapter available, any of the older HP ScanJet
units work great. I can vouch for the IIcx. It's like having an
aircraft carrier on your desk, but they're fully supported by Sane,
and can usually be found for ~$20 on eBay or Craigslist.

<http://www.sane-project.org/sane-mfgs.html#Z-HEWLETT-PACKARD>

-- 
Paul

Just to clarify... The Fireside gang are called PDXLUG, not PDXLinux.
The domain name for the PLUG is PDXLinux.Org.  PDXLUG is *not* the
Portland Linux User Group or the Portland Linux/Unix Group.

PDXLUG is (descriptively) another Linux user group in Portland, but
PDXLUG just stands for PDXLUG.  When you want to refer to PDXLUG,
please refer to them as PDXLUG.  It will help to minimize the
inevitable confusion.

PLUG *is* either the Portland Linux User Group or, more frequently
these days, the Portland Linux/Unix Group.

Yes, this is tedious.  Yes, they've been asked to change their name to
something less confusing.  No, sadly, their isn't any obvious progress
in that direction.

Is it time for a "reading archaic media" party? 

Dave and Alan can read 9 track tape, I have a couple of tapes.
I can bring some SCSI Exabyte drives, 2GB/5GB, if somebody can
talk to the SCSI.  I can also bring some SCSI drives that read
ancient DC600 tapes, and an older Iomega drive or two.  If pressed,
I could even bring a minitower with an Adaptec SCSI card and
CD burner and monitor and keyboard, but that is more work ... 

Now if somebody can read Tektronix-formatted DC300 tapes, or the
old small-spool DEC tapes, or Commodore cassette, or 8 inch CPM
floppy, or papertape, or punchcard, I can read ALL the old media I
have squirreled away for the last 40 years.  I do have options for
papertape and punchcard though;  I can read those visually ... :-)

Often the problem is not reading the raw media, but interpreting
the bytes on them into modern data files.  Is there a CPAN module
for converting Baudot or EBCDIC into ASCII?  Howsabout cuneiform?

I can imagine Randal organizing a "read old media" cruise.  We
show up with our old media and old equipment, spend the cruise
time reading and converting our old media, and when we are done
we pitch the old media over the side ...  :-)

Keith

From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu May 11 14:33:29 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Thu, 11 May 2006 14:33:29 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Case-insensitivity and GNU make
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0605111412230.4497@arrowhead.galois.com>

My personal web site contains a few technical articles, the sources 
for which are marked up in DocBook.

The top level directory looks like this:

   CVS/               index.html              sendmail-auth/
   Makefile           index.xml               sendmail-genericstable/
   apache-fp/         ldap-aliases/           soho-bind/
   apache-google/     open-letter-microsoft/  sort-addr/
   dhcp-failover/     openssl/                specs/
   dig/               pine-macosx/            trac-centos/
   exim4-ssl-client/  pine-ssl/               utf8/
   gpg-quickstart/    rcs/

Revision control is provided by CVS -- hence the CVS directory -- but 
I have an article stored in a directory called "rcs"; it contains a 
Makefile and an XML source doc (and current, an HTML target doc):

   CVS/   Makefile   index.html   index.xml

I recently moved my main workstation to an Intel-based Mac mini, so I 
rsync-ed my projects tree from my Linux box to the Mac. I've had 
occasion to build files in the subdirectories of the source tree 
listed above, but until today I hadn't had to run make in the 
top-level directory. Oops:

   [heinlein]$ make
   co  RCS/index.html index.html
   co: RCS/index.html:1: missing 'head' keyword
   co aborted
   make: *** [index.html] Error 1

To make a long story short, GNU make has an implicit rule that looks 
for %,v or RCS/% or RCS/%,v. If it finds one of those, it will try to 
checkout the file before executing the target commands. You can get a 
list of all the implicit rules by asking nicely:

   make -p -f/dev/null 2>&1 | less

The Mac's filesystem is case-insensitive, so it interpreted the 
presence of the "rcs" directory just the same as "RCS" -- so it tried 
to checkout rcs/index.html. Since that's not a real RCS file, it 
failed.

Ugh. Silly Macintosh.

There are a couple solutions:

1. Turn off all explicit rules from the command line:

    make -r

2. In the Makefile redefine the offending rule(s) as no-ops. This
    is the tack I chose:

    # override gnu make's auto-checkout feature. bad mojo here...
    %:: %,v
    %:: RCS/%,v
    %:: RCS/%

    # rest of makefile ...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

> On Thu, 2006-05-11 at 14:33 -0700, Paul Heinlein wrote:
>> The Mac's filesystem is case-insensitive, so it interpreted the

>> failed.

> I thought OS X could also do UFS/FFS?  At least, NeXTSTEP 3.3 did... ;)

There's an option for case-sensitivity, but it's not the default and 
Macs don't ship with it. It's relatively easy to create a disk image 
with a sane filesystem and mount it; I just haven't yet done so at 
home.

There are some reports of certain Mac applications (Photoshop being 
one) behaving badly on case-sensitive filesystems -- but I've never 
seen such things first hand.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

Yep. Mention was made here of a Hewlett-Packard IIcx. That is exactly
the scanner I have used on my Windows desktop(s) since I bought it new
during previous millennium. It works, but has always been flaky about
the SCSI bus. Sometimes I have to restart the scanning or copy utility
repeatedly before it sees the scanner. Sometimes it will just stop in
the middle of a scan. Restarting the scan usually works, but sometimes
I have to restart the copy or scan utility.

In contrast, the USB 2.0 connection from my Ubuntu-64 laptop to my
Canon LiDE 30 works first time, every time. A bit on the slow side, but
that is probably a USB issue. However, while the connection to the
Canon is far better under Linux, OCR is far better under Windows.

I just wanted to add my voice to the chorus of complaints about SCSI
connections to HP scanners. HP's SCSI scanner connections suck.

AS939SLI32-eSATA2 M1695 939 motherboard, an Athlon 64 3000+ 1.8G 939P R
processor, two 250Gb Western Digital SATA2 hard drives which I've set up as
a mirror pair.  I've followed the istructions at
http://www.gentoo.org/doc/en/handbook/handbook-amd64.xml as carefully as I
know how.  When configuring the kernel, I used genkernel so as to include
drivers for as may devices as possible.

While I'm doing the install, everything appears to work.  Files appear to be
copied to the hard drive, and I can hear the hard drives working.  This
makes me think my hardware is OK.  When I get to the very end and reboot,
grub loads (suggesting to me that the hard drives are working after the
reboot) and a bunch of drivers load (suggesting to me that the boot sector
at /dev/sda1 is working).  Then I get a series of messages:

>>Activating udev
>>Determining root device...
!!Block device /dev/sda3 is not a valid root device...
!!The root block device is unspecified or not detected.
Please specify a device to boot, or "shell" for a shell...
boot() ::

If I type "shell" at the prompt, I see:

boot() :: shell

BusyBox v1.00 (2006.05.04-22:57+0000) Built-in shell (ash)
Enter 'help' for a list of built-in commands.

/bin/ash: cant access tty; job control turned off
/ #

Typing "help" gets:

/ # help

Built-in commands:
________________
    . : alias bg break cd chdir continue eval exec exit export false
    fg hash help jobs kill let local pwd read readonly return set
    shift times trap true type ulimit umask unalies unset wait

I have discovered that /usr/bin, /sbin, and /bin exist and are on the path,
and the commands I have tried from those directories seem to work.

Patterned after code listing 4 on this page
http://www.gentoo.org/doc/en/handbook/handbook-amd64.xml?part=1&chap=10,
these commands are in my grub.conf:

root (hd0,0)
kernel /boot/kernel-genkernel-x86_64-2.6.15-gentoo-r7 root=/dev/ram0
init=/linuxrc ramdisk=8192 real_root=/dev/sda3 udev
initrd /boot/initramfs-genkernel-x86_64-2.6.15-gentoo-r7

I really feel like I must be very close to a working system.  Any
suggestions of what I need to do next?

Dan Treadway

> a mirror pair.  I've followed the istructions at

What type of mirror?  Using Linux software raid or the alleged
hardware raid on the motherboard?  If it is the former, then your root
device will probably be something other than /dev/sda3 (like /dev/md0
or however that works these days).  If the latter, then I'm guessing
the motherboard "raid" only works in Windows as it is "fake-raid".
See http://linux-ata.org/ for more info and status of ULi chipset,
which looks to be what your board has.

> http://www.gentoo.org/doc/en/handbook/handbook-amd64.xml as carefully as I
> know how.  When configuring the kernel, I used genkernel so as to include
> drivers for as may devices as possible.

What happens if you type "fdisk -l" at the busybox prompt?  Do you see
one hard drive (/dev/sda) or two (/dev/sda and /dev/sdb)?

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Sat May 13 12:18:37 2006
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Sat, 13 May 2006 12:18:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] [amesfug] still trying to load linux (fwd)
In-Reply-To: <DNEBLHLLGBDHPEBCGMIPIEOCENAA.treadway@netins.net>
References: <c29537840605131027t5df9635dr4adb5d54eb501ff0@mail.gmail.com>
	<DNEBLHLLGBDHPEBCGMIPIEOCENAA.treadway@netins.net>
Message-ID: <c29537840605131218r4c0db846hfa12594ed1c928c6@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/13/06, Dan Treadway <treadway at netins.net> wrote:
> Jason,
>
> I was trying to use the motherboard's RAID.
>
> mount does not show hard drives loaded.
>
> fdisk comes back
>
> /bin/ask: fdisk: not found

Okay, apparently the ramdisk does not have fdisk.  Try running fdisk
from your install disk or another bootable CD (like Knoppix).  The
goal is to determine whether your RAID is working.  If you see only
one drive, then either the other drive isn't working or the RAID is
working transparently (I would be surprised).  If you see two drives
then it is as I expect, and you have fake-raid.

> Do you suggest I break the motherboard mirror?  That done, would I need to
> start completely over, or could the work already done be preserved?

Assuming you have fake-raid, I would recommend using the software RAID
Linux provides (assuming you still want mirroring).  The performance
is excellent and you are less likely to run into strange problems
later.  That said, I have not used RAID of any kind for a while, so
there may be others on this list who feel like adding their input.

Ultimately the amount of effort to get it right the first time is
worth the long-term reliability.  Out of curiosity, when you created
the filesystem(s) (mkfs), what device(s) did you provide to it?
/dev/sda3?  Other (non-Gentoo) distros will likely have an installer
that would set up the software RAID for you.  The choice of distro is
yours to make of course :-)

Jason "haven't used Gentoo in 3 years" Martin

Mel wrote:

> Did you empty the Recycle Bin on the Win98 machine? If not, I think you 
> have the option to restore the file.

Thanks for the thought, but Win98 doesn't store deleted files in the 
Recycle Bin if those files were on another machine. There is an updated 
version of Samba that adds that capability, but I have not installed it.

Once deleted, they are "gone" in the sense that they are not visible to 
the file system utilities, like Explorer. However, just as in Windows, 
they are sitting there on the drive unchanged, but just unreferenced. I 
don't work at that level often enough to remember what Linux calls the 
little boxes on the hard drive that hold parts of files and, more 
importantly, how to look at them and reclaim them before they are reused 
for some other file. Even back in the days of DOS, there was a Norton 
tool to recover erased files simply by changing the first character in 
the filename from (something that meant unused) back to what it used to 
be. There's a similar concept in Linux (although not quite that simple) 
but I can't remember what it's called so I can go read the man page 
about how to recover the deleted files.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

On Sun, 14 May 2006, Robert Kopp wrote:

>> Both OS are on separate disks (so hd0 is Linux and hd1 is WinXP).

> You're booting from the second hard drive. I think GRUB has another command

   It used to be -- with earlier versions of Microsoft -- that it would boot
only from the first hard drive. Try Tim's suggestion, or rejumper the drives
(and alter mount points) so the XP is hd0 and linux is on hd1.

From nixlists at writemoore.net  Mon May 15 09:23:50 2006

Subject: [PLUG] fstab hell(p)
Message-ID: <4468AB16.8080500@writemoore.net>

I've become hopelessly muddled on how properly to set-up an ext3 
partition I want to share between Linux OS'es.

I set up one large ext3 partition when I reinstalled Debian.  The fstab 
line is:

/dev/sdb5       /mnt/library    ext3    defaults        0       2

I want this to be automounted when I boot into either Linux OS, which is 
happening.  But only root can write to it, since root is the owner.  How 
do I allow my user accounts in each OS read & write access?  I use the 
same username and UID in each OS, so there shouldn't be any file 
ownership conflicts when I go from one to the other.

Every bit of googling and every printed reference I have (including the 
Linux Cookbook) turns up how to accomplish this (or similar) for 
accessing Windows  or vfat partitions, but I can't find the right 
entries to do it for an ext3 partition and I can't parse man mount or 
man fstab well enough to figure it out.  UID doesn't work, it's not a 
recognized option for ext3.  I know one way to do it is to change the 
ownership of the whole partition, but I thought that was a security 
risk.  Am I wrong about that?

On Mon, 15 May 2006, Michael M. wrote:

> I set up one large ext3 partition when I reinstalled Debian.  The fstab line 
> is:
>
> /dev/sdb5       /mnt/library    ext3    defaults        0       2

> I want this to be automounted when I boot into either Linux OS, which is 
> happening.  But only root can write to it, since root is the owner.  How do I 
> allow my user accounts in each OS read & write access?

   On Slackware and reiserfs or ext3 (two different hosts here), 'defaults'
includes users and read-write priviledges. If I was at your console, I'd
modify /etc/fstabs so that line reads
         defaults,users,rw

and see if that fixes the issue with explicit instructions. Please report
your findings.

Rich

From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Mon May 15 11:17:53 2006
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Mon, 15 May 2006 11:17:53 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] fstab hell(p)
In-Reply-To: <4468C1E6.1000606@writemoore.net>
References: <4468AB16.8080500@writemoore.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0605150955270.31334@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4468C1E6.1000606@writemoore.net>
Message-ID: <20060515181753.GB21086@nellump.ath.cx>

On Mon, May 15, 2006 at 11:01:10AM -0700, Michael M. wrote:

> Rich Shepard wrote:
> If I was at your console, I'd
> >modify /etc/fstabs so that line reads defaults,users,rw

The "user" option only allows non-root users to mount and unmount a
file system. It doesn't affect file system permissions.

> There has to be a way to do this, yes? Or is the only way to change
> the ownership? One reference I have says that you should *not*
> change the ownership of /mnt or any top-level partitions. But how do
> I write to the damn partition then?

I think you're getting your terminology a bit confused. You're trying
to mount a file system (that lives on a hard disk partition). The
file system gets mounted to a mount point, which is just another
directory on an already mounted file system.

Have you looked at the permissions set on the file system you're
trying to mount? Not the permissions of the mount point (those get
overridden once the file system is mounted), but the permissions of
the file system itself.

There's probably no need to change the ownership of the root of the
file system itself (that would be the mount point /mnt/whatever
*after* you've mounted the file system). It might be the easiest
route, though. I can't think of any fundamental security concern
beyond allowing everyone else on the system to read your files.

-- 
Paul

From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Mon May 15 12:27:23 2006
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Mon, 15 May 2006 12:27:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] fstab hell(p)
In-Reply-To: <4468CE87.7000501@writemoore.net>
References: <4468AB16.8080500@writemoore.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0605150955270.31334@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4468C1E6.1000606@writemoore.net>
	<20060515181753.GB21086@nellump.ath.cx>
	<4468CE87.7000501@writemoore.net>
Message-ID: <20060515192723.GA21575@nellump.ath.cx>

On Mon, May 15, 2006 at 11:55:03AM -0700, Michael M. wrote:

> How do I look at the permissions set on the file system? I don't 
> understand the distinction between the permissions of a mount point, a 
> directory, a file, and a file system.

A mount point is just a directory on another file system (any file
system that isn't the one you're trying to mount). Typically, mount
points live on the root file system. The mount point, being just
another directory, has its own permissions settings, but these don't
have any effect on what you can and cannot do to a file system once
it's been mounted there. Once you've actually mounted a file system to
a mount point, the permissions that used to be set on the mount point
(when it was just a lonely directory) magically disappear, and the
permissions of the root level of the mounted file system take their
place. The mounted file system effectively "masks off" the original
directory being used as the mount point.

> The fstab entry, currently, is:
> /dev/sdb1 /mnt/library ext3 defaults,users,rw,noauto 0 2

Unless you want to be able to mount and unmount the "library" file
system as a normal (non-root) user, then you don't neet the "user"
option in your fstab (it's "user", not "users"). And if you want the
file system automatically mounted when the system starts up, then
remove the "noauto" option. Finally, "defaults" implies "rw", so it's
not necessary to include. But it looks like you may know all this
already.

> But when I try to create a file as a user, I get 'permission denied.'

That's because only root has write permissions to the filesystem. If
you don't have a firm grasp on *nix file system permissions, the LDP
has decent overview in their intro guide:
<http://www.tldp.org/LDP/intro-linux/html/sect_03_04.html>

> So if I change the ownership of /mnt/library to my own user and group, I 
> would be able to write to the directory or partition or filesystem or 
> however you want to phrase it?

Yes, that would work, *if* you make those changes after the file
system has been mounted.

Try this, as root, with the file system mounted:
1) cd /mnt/library
2) mkdir mikes_stuff
3) chown mcubed. mikes_stuff

Your normal user account should be able to read and write to the new
"mikes_stuff" directory.

-- 
Paul


From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Mon May 15 12:43:07 2006

	<20060515192723.GA21575@nellump.ath.cx>
Message-ID: <20060515194307.GB22779@nellump.ath.cx>

On Mon, May 15, 2006 at 12:27:23PM -0700, Paul Mullen wrote:
 
> Try this, as root, with the file system mounted:
> 1) cd /mnt/library
> 2) mkdir mikes_stuff
> 3) chown mcubed. mikes_stuff

Additionally, you could also just perform step #3 on /mnt/library
(again, *after* you've mounted the file system). It's mostly just a
matter of personal preference. You should not have to adjust the
permissions of /mnt. It does need to be "executable" by any user that
wishes to access the lower levels (such as /mnt/library) but that
should be the default (unless someone's changed it).

"ls -l /" should have an entry for "mnt" similar to the following:
   drwxr-xr-x    4 root root 4.0K Jan 19 03:12 mnt/

-- 
Paul

Michael M. <nixlists at writemoore.net> wrote:

> Paul Mullen wrote:
> > On Mon, May 15, 2006 at 11:55:03AM -0700, Michael M. wrote:
> >
> >
> >> How do I look at the permissions set on the file system? I don't
> >> understand the distinction between the permissions of a mount
> >> point, a directory, a file, and a file system.
> >>
> >
> > A mount point is just a directory on another file system (any file
> > system that isn't the one you're trying to mount). Typically, mount
> > points live on the root file system. The mount point, being just
> > another directory, has its own permissions settings, but these don't
> > have any effect on what you can and cannot do to a file system once
> > it's been mounted there. Once you've actually mounted a file system to
> > a mount point, the permissions that used to be set on the mount point
> > (when it was just a lonely directory) magically disappear, and the
> > permissions of the root level of the mounted file system take their
> > place. The mounted file system effectively "masks off" the original
> > directory being used as the mount point.
> >
> I follow all of that up until "the permissions of the root level of
> the mounted file system take their place." I still don't understand
> how a "file system" has permissions. Maybe it's just the terminology
> that is confusing me. I thought directories and files have
> permissions. I thought a "file system" referred to a file system type
> (ext3, ReiserFS, xfs, ntfs, etc.), and as such had no particular
> permissions.

It's not a file system permission.  The "root level of the mounted
filesystem" is just a directory.  The permissions of that directory
control what is allowed.  An equivalent would be the permissions
on /

galen

John (hi!),

Ubuntu isn't an rpm-based system. For a list of all mysql-related 
packages, use aptitude:

   aptitude search mysql

In this case, you'll probably want to install the mysql virtual 
package:

   sudo apt-get install mysql

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

Sadly, along with XP: 98Se 98, probably ME, etc. are not easily updated anymore.
Why did Microsoft do this?  There are a lot of computers around that won't
run XP let alone anything else that is more recent.  If Microsoft really wants
to tick people off, it should release a new Windows variant that won't run on
most of the computers around.  Sure, break driver and software compatibility
again in mass.  When you realize that Windows programs are easily $29 a piece 
or more on average, losing a library of such programs could prove quite 
painful. 

Are there are any reasonable OSS clones of old versions of Windows?  I like
Freedos, but when I tried to install 98 second edition over it hoping I'd
be able to use either system off of the same partition, I ran into major 
problems.  My Freedos image is the beta 9 release, don't know if it ever
got out of beta.

What I don't care for is that Windows is getting more bloated and Microsoft
is getting more aggressive about pushing people to buy new releases.  It 
used to be with 98 update cd's for example that you only needed one file in
particular to trick the copy protection.  If I ran Microsoft, I'd release the
next version of Windows under the GPL and simply keep minor parts of the OS
proprietary.  At least I'd release the updates under a license that is similar
to the GPL.  The public should not have to pay for Microsoft's programming
mistakes nor should the public lose access to critical updates because 
Microsoft doesn't feel like providing them anymore.

I'm wondering how Linspire and Xandros are doing, but it appears that these
are purchase only systems and that there isn't an evaluation version.  Vmware
is nice, but I get tired of worrying about licenses with actual Microsoft
products even under vmware.  I'm stuck getting evaluation keys for vmware
because I can't afford the license yet.

I bet I'm not alone in wondering why I should buy another computer so that I 
can run Vista.  XP has only been around for 4 years, hasn't it?

Why isn't Novell putting out it's own competitive desktop product to Windows?
Novell's networking code is traditionally better than Microsoft's.  If Novell
would open source NDS, it would really fly as a samba alternative.  Novell
could probably do well supporting their code even after open sourcing it.
Imagine Windows programs redesigned to easily abstract to competitor network
protocols, being able to use decent networking code with Windows programs
would be a major plus.

Is anyone doing anything in particular to acquire the major updates for end
of life versions of Windows?  

Michael C. Robinson

From max.murphy at gmail.com  Thu May 11 14:45:18 2006
From: max.murphy at gmail.com (Max Murphy)
Date: Thu, 11 May 2006 14:45:18 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Case-insensitivity and GNU make
In-Reply-To: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0605111412230.4497@arrowhead.galois.com>
References: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0605111412230.4497@arrowhead.galois.com>
Message-ID: <2BA29F33-AF38-4DA0-80B8-63CB890A8F07@maxmurphy.net>

Paul,

I saw your post and have another possible solution for your mac  
issues with case insensitivity.

Use Disk Utility to create a blank disk image, then format it with  
"UNIX Filesystem" under the erase tab. You could then use that disk  
image which will mount under /Volumes/[disk image name]/ to house  
your repository on your mac. I had to do this quite a few times  
before just having a dedicated partition with this filesystem.

Hope this might help.

Max Murphy


On May 11, 2006, at 2:33 PM, Paul Heinlein wrote:

> My personal web site contains a few technical articles, the sources  
> for which are marked up in DocBook.
>
> The top level directory looks like this:
>
>   CVS/               index.html              sendmail-auth/
>   Makefile           index.xml               sendmail-genericstable/
>   apache-fp/         ldap-aliases/           soho-bind/
>   apache-google/     open-letter-microsoft/  sort-addr/
>   dhcp-failover/     openssl/                specs/
>   dig/               pine-macosx/            trac-centos/
>   exim4-ssl-client/  pine-ssl/               utf8/
>   gpg-quickstart/    rcs/
>
> Revision control is provided by CVS -- hence the CVS directory --  
> but I have an article stored in a directory called "rcs"; it  
> contains a Makefile and an XML source doc (and current, an HTML  
> target doc):
>
>   CVS/   Makefile   index.html   index.xml
>
> I recently moved my main workstation to an Intel-based Mac mini, so  
> I rsync-ed my projects tree from my Linux box to the Mac. I've had  
> occasion to build files in the subdirectories of the source tree  
> listed above, but until today I hadn't had to run make in the top- 
> level directory. Oops:
>
>   [heinlein]$ make
>   co  RCS/index.html index.html
>   co: RCS/index.html:1: missing 'head' keyword
>   co aborted
>   make: *** [index.html] Error 1
>
> To make a long story short, GNU make has an implicit rule that  
> looks for %,v or RCS/% or RCS/%,v. If it finds one of those, it  
> will try to checkout the file before executing the target commands.  
> You can get a list of all the implicit rules by asking nicely:
>
>   make -p -f/dev/null 2>&1 | less
>
> The Mac's filesystem is case-insensitive, so it interpreted the  
> presence of the "rcs" directory just the same as "RCS" -- so it  
> tried to checkout rcs/index.html. Since that's not a real RCS file,  
> it failed.
>
> Ugh. Silly Macintosh.
>
> There are a couple solutions:
>
> 1. Turn off all explicit rules from the command line:
>
>    make -r
>
> 2. In the Makefile redefine the offending rule(s) as no-ops. This
>    is the tack I chose:
>
>    # override gnu make's auto-checkout feature. bad mojo here...
>    %:: %,v
>    %:: RCS/%,v
>    %:: RCS/%
>
>    # rest of makefile ...
>
> -- 
> Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com
>
>
>
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

I just had to reply, sorry =^)

plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> Sadly, along with XP: 98Se 98, probably ME, etc. are not easily updated anymore.
> Why did Microsoft do this?

So you wouldn't feel remorse when you switched to Linux or MacOs

> There are a lot of computers around that won't
> run XP let alone anything else that is more recent.  If Microsoft really wants
> to tick people off, it should release a new Windows variant that won't run on
> most of the computers around.

Microsoft does just that, yet another reason to switch to something which 
truly is stable - open source that can constantly be adjusted to fit the 
hardware that is out there and still work on older computers.

> Sure, break driver and software compatibility
> again in mass.  When you realize that Windows programs are easily $29 a piece 
> or more on average, losing a library of such programs could prove quite 
> painful. 
> 
> Are there are any reasonable OSS clones of old versions of Windows?

Fortunately not. Most people realized that it's much harder to clone something 
closed source and open source OS's out there are far more powerfull anyway, 
and they didn't stray long before turning to the light side ;^)

Some fail to give up the dark side and work on a Windows NT clone, in the hope 
that one day the dark side will... prevail

> I like
> Freedos, but when I tried to install 98 second edition over it hoping I'd
> be able to use either system off of the same partition, I ran into major 
> problems.

of course, freedos is """compatible""" but up to certain limits - it doesn't 
replace the underlying OS of windows 98.

 > If I ran Microsoft, I'd release the
> next version of Windows under the GPL and simply keep minor parts of the OS
> proprietary.

I'm not sure whether I would buy your stock then - but with any OSS-aspiring 
OS out there, it's dubious what is the better investment in the long run when 
it comes to closed source. My bet is a fully open source OS and closed source 
applications for the enterprise, but even that sounds like a tough battle...

> At least I'd release the updates under a license that is similar
> to the GPL. 

the problem with that is viral licensing effects of the GPL - once released 
you might accidentally open source closed source components - very tricky and 
lots of lawyers earn their pay from research of these type of issues these 
days ;^)

> The public should not have to pay for Microsoft's programming
> mistakes nor should the public lose access to critical updates because 
> Microsoft doesn't feel like providing them anymore.

I dunno, the problem is that you didn't buy a car that was defective, and can 
demand a free repair. It's more like you bought a book, and there were typo's 
in it. If you used the book for training or worse - teaching, then you do have 
a problem when mistakes in it don't get fixed. Unfortunately the seller of the 
book may not see a financial gain in fixing the mistakes, so you end up 
editing the book yourself. The worst part is just that you don't have the 
print of the book, since it's closed source - but hey - that's what you bought 
in the first place, and you paid money for too.

I hope you get the idea...

> I'm wondering how Linspire and Xandros are doing, but it appears that these
> are purchase only systems and that there isn't an evaluation version.  Vmware
> is nice, but I get tired of worrying about licenses with actual Microsoft
> products even under vmware.  I'm stuck getting evaluation keys for vmware
> because I can't afford the license yet.

vmware player is free, and the latest beta workstation is up for grabs too 
now, no license fees.

> I bet I'm not alone in wondering why I should buy another computer so that I 
> can run Vista.  XP has only been around for 4 years, hasn't it?

I stopped worrying about windows about 10 years ago, only using windows when 
my employer(s) really honestly need me to use windows applications, and at 
work. saves me lots of grey hairs...

> Why isn't Novell putting out it's own competitive desktop product to Windows?

it's called novell desktop linux ;^)

> Novell's networking code is traditionally better than Microsoft's.  If Novell
> would open source NDS, it would really fly as a samba alternative.  Novell
> could probably do well supporting their code even after open sourcing it.

probably encumbered by patents and crosslicensing - I doubt that will ever 
happen, but I have no idea about NDS really, I'm just guessing.

> Is anyone doing anything in particular to acquire the major updates for end
> of life versions of Windows?  

not me ;^)

Cheers,

Auke

Since Aaron has not announced this yet, I will unofficially do so:
------------------------------------------------------------------

PLUG members have teamed up with Free Geek to provide a Linux Clinic.

Every third Sunday of the month, from 1PM to 5PM, folks are invited
to bring their Linux questions (and answers), Linux computers (and
wanna-bes) or even just their curiosity, to:

   FREE GEEK
   Community Technology Center
   1731 SE 10th Avenue
   Portland, OR 97214
   503-232-9350

Directions here: http://www.freegeek.org/map.php
Map: http://maps.google.com/maps?f=q&hl=en&q=1731+SE+10th+Avenue+97214

NOTE: Free Geek is closed during the Linux Clinic, and they won't
be answering the phones.  Please enter through the south door,
next to the Thrift Store.  We'll have a sign by the door.

The next few clinics are on:
 May    21
 June   18
 July   16
 August 20
---------------------------------------------------------------------

possible for discs that fail a security check to trigger a notification

"update" that would actually render the player useless..."

I am looking at capturing data from one of these nice Fluke 189
Digital Multimeters.  This particular one has an infrared interface
and an optional cable to connect to a USB port so that you can
interrogate the device for its current reading (our data collection
protocol currently has us entering an unstable value by hand, which is
proving to be quite tedious).

           ir        ir    usb+---------------+
 [Fluke189]-( ~~~~~~ )------[=| laptop        |
                     ir189usb +---------------+

Does anyone have experience with this cable, and do you know if it
works with recent linux kernels.  It is probably some kind of
usb-serial device inside, but I haven't had my hands on one yet (got
one on order).  I've found the protocol for querying the device, so it
really just boils down to the details of being able to talk across the
link.

TIA.

From ehem at m5p.com  Sun May 21 14:11:16 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sun, 21 May 2006 14:11:16 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] External USB 2.0 hard drives
In-Reply-To: <20060430190004.85CB445D0@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200605212111.k4LLBGwo020300@m5p.com>

>From: "Michael M." <nixlists at writemoore.net>
> I can understand any hardware manufacturers not wanting to make it sound 
> like they will support 200 different Linux distros, but honestly how 
> difficult would it be for them to include a brief footnote that a 
> particular piece of equipment should work without problems on any Linux 
> OS with USB support?  It's frustrating that so often they won't even 
> provide that kind of basic information.  It's when I see "Supported 
> Operation Sytems: Window blah blah Macintosh blah" and no Linux that I 
> have niggling doubts.  It's yet another factor that slows Linux adoption 
> because it's another case of expecting Linux users to be able to read 
> between the lines and "understand" that, yeah, this is fine despite the 
> refusal to spell that out, or no, this will be a problem because of x y 
> or z.

If they support MacOS, that is a very hopeful sign for working with
Linux. Mainly the most common mistakes with implementing the USB-storage
standard also cause problems for MacOS. This won't rule out firmware bugs
such as the one on found on Conner 1060 drives which caused problems for
ext2 (and *only* ext2!), but is still a good sign.


>From: Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com>
> I'm pretty sure the USB mass-storage protocol is simple and stable 
> enough that no manufacturer besides olympus has managed to screw it up 
> (and that was a camera.)

Simple, stable, and easy to screw up. Part of the problem was the EvilOS
driver stack for USB-storage was written from scratch, not sharing any
code with either their IDE or SCSI stacks. As a result they didn't
exercise things like multisector transfers nor command queueing. MacOS
and Linux though place USB-storage on top of their SCSI stacks, as a
result both will attempt to utilize those capabilities if the drive
indicates support.

If they only test for working with EvilOS, those flags won't be looked at
and may break other OSes (including embedded OSes on various bits of
hardware). This sort of breakage is also an issue with 1394/FireWire
devices. I was talking to an Apple Engineer at one point and he said this
was a favorite problem.

From ehem at m5p.com  Sun May 21 16:00:16 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sun, 21 May 2006 16:00:16 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <20060508190007.ABF3744BC@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200605212300.k4LN0Gc5021139@m5p.com>

>From: Bruce Kilpatrick <bakilpatrick at verizon.net>
> I have been watching this thread and wondering why I hadn't seen this
> forced fsck happen.  It finally occured to me...I rarely shut my Ubuntu
> box off.  It hasn't seen 30 boots since I installed Ubuntu on it.
> Judging by what I am reading here, maybe I should manually cause the
> system to run fsck once in a while???

If you keep it running long enough, you'll go into the 180 days forced
fsck. With the automatic checks in place, no need.


>From: m0gely <m0gely at telestream.com>
> I've been watching this too and am puzzled.  What's the point at all? 
> If the system is being shutdown properly then fsck shouldn't be needed 
> on boot outside of special scenarios.  BSD doesn't do this.

Even with hard shutdowns it shouldn't be needed, as the journal will
contain everything needed to restore the filesystem to a consistant
state.

This though assumes your hardware works perfectly and the filesystem code
doesn't have any sort of bugs. Alas it is rare for either of these to be
true, so the forced checks are an attempt to compensate for hardware and
software that aren't perfect. Even with ECC memory you have to watch out
for bit-rot on the filesystem.

Then there's the question of blanks:  aren't Blu-ray blanks close to $50 
each?  I haven't heard how much HD-DVD blanks are going for, if there 
are any available yet.  I wonder if it wouldn't be cheaper, per GB, to 
use hard drives rather than these discs, for the foreseeable future.  
I'd also be worried about the media's reliability, at least until some 
tests are done.

I wonder if either format will really take off the way DVD did.  I 
suspect a lot of people are perfectly happy with DVDs as they are, and 
the future may lie with Internet distribution rather than overpriced 
shiny plastic discs that get tossed into landfills.  But I'm not exactly 
the market for this stuff since I don't play games and the only TV I 
have is a 12-in analog portable that is sitting in the back of the closet.

-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

From dexter at ambidexter.com  Tue May 23 06:51:55 2006
From: dexter at ambidexter.com (Michael Dexter)
Date: Tue, 23 May 2006 16:51:55 +0300
Subject: [PLUG] OpenBSD sysjail announcement
Message-ID: <a062309a4c098c0f2e24c@[192.168.15.108]>


Sveiki PLUGinieki!

Some of you may be interested in a project I have been working on 
with a developer here in Riga while in voluntary exile.

It is a more or less drop-in replacement for FreeBSD jail but using 
Systrace on OpenBSD. This can give you either an improved chroot or 
system virtualization without surrendering significant system 
resources to Xen, UserModeLinux, VMWare and the like.

I welcome any experienced OpenBSD users (or frustrated FreeBSD users) 
to try it, punch holes in it and let me know how we can improve it 
prior to official release.

http://sysjail.bsd.lv

 From the web page:

sysjail is a userland virtualisation system for operating systems 
supporting the systrace library. It is developed on and currently 
supports OpenBSD. The first generation of sysjail is as close to a 
drop-in replacement for FreeBSD's jail subsystem as permitted. As 
with a jail, processes within a sysjail are disallowed resources 
belonging to the system proper, known as the "host" system, or other 
sysjail instances. While sysjail currently behaves as an OpenBSD 
implementation of jail, it has the potential to provide significant 
auditing, profiling and debugging tools allowing full auditing of 
intercepted system calls, run-time modification and extension of 
policies, etc. The sysjail sources are released mainly under 
OpenBSD's 3-part license.

Please say "hello" to FUP and have a Swagat lunch special for me.

Take care,

Michael Dexter


From sofar at foo-projects.org  Tue May 23 08:13:42 2006

On Tue, 23 May 2006 03:49:42 -0700 (PDT), linux at frankhunt.com wrote:
> I am using Comcast cable modem with a Linksys WRT54G router and want to
> use DDNS for web and email.  I have opened up ports 80 and 25 and mapped
> them to my web/email server NAT'd IP.  How can I test the email part
> without first setting up the DDNS service?  The web works fine at
> http://nnn.nnn.nnn.nnn to a dummy main page.  Shouldn't I be able to send
> email to user@[nnn.nnn.nnn.nnn] ?  

most smtp's will never send to numeric ip addresses as a precaution. if you really need to do this then you need to setup an smtp that explicitly allows this and will relay mail for you - you probably don't want to go there.

If you can get some dyndns service up you might be able to use the @host.dyndnsservice.whatever hostname instead, providing MX records are setup OK.

Auke

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue May 23 16:10:26 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 23 May 2006 16:10:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Dynamic DNS Stuff
In-Reply-To: <a84585a6652d7dd111f99b37cad36b14@localhost>
References: <35092.68.62.93.55.1148381382.squirrel@www.frankhunt.com>
	<a84585a6652d7dd111f99b37cad36b14@localhost>
Message-ID: <20060523231026.GA26704@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Tue, May 23, 2006 at 03:13:42PM +0000, Auke Kok wrote:

> If you can get some dyndns service up you might be able to use the @host.dyndnsservice.whatever hostname instead, providing MX records are setup OK.
> 
> Auke

Or more precisely, dyndns.com will give you up to 5 free subdomains, so
you can register with them and set up a DNS entry for hunt.dyndns.com
or frank.homelinux.com , for example.   That is good for mail testing,
*assuming* you have your SMTP server configured to respond to that
domain name.  

Note that a reverse dns query will not respond properly, and when
somebody does a reverse dns lookup on your IP address, they will
get something like c-67-171-176-23.hsd1.or.comcast.net.   Many
mailers will not talk to you if they see an RDNS entry to a dynamic
address pool like that ( mine won't, you probable spammer, you!  :-/ ).

You will want an honest-to-linus static IP address, and a reverse
DNS entry served by the machines in charge of that address block,
if you want all mailers to talk to you.  My offsite virtual server
is at 207.210.219.119, and a dig -x 207.210.219.119 reveals:

;; ANSWER SECTION:
119.219.210.207.in-addr.arpa. 3592 IN   PTR     kl-ic.com.

So I get all mail addressed to kl-ic.com., almost all mail addressed
to keithl.com (because I have a legitimate static address), and miss
some from very strict mailers that want proper RDNS even from static
addresses.  

One of the cheaper ways to get a static IP address and oodles
of bandwidth is an offsite virtual colocation server.  See my
presentation to the April PLUG advanced topics meeting at  
http://www.keithl.com/offsite.html .  You also don't bend your
Comcast terms of service and risk getting your ports blocked
or your service terminated.  

Finally, think twice before setting up your own mail domain.  When
the spammers find the domain, you will have a LOT of new friends.
I get about 30 spam attempts per minute, increasing at 15% a month.
One of the reasons for an offsite is so that I can deal with them
before they impact my tiny comcast 768K inbound bandwidth.  I have
lots of spam filtering at the offsite server, so less heads down 
the thin pipe.

Keith

PS:  it is common to obscure IP addresses in examples like this, 
but silly when a ping to keithl.com gives my IP address in seconds.

PPS:
> Frank Hunt
> Befuddled SysAdmin
> Sterling Heights, MI

we miss?

From krisa at subtend.net  Tue May 23 17:55:00 2006
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Tue, 23 May 2006 17:55:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ISP/Static IP for SOHOs
In-Reply-To: <97aa5afe0605231613l7fd8a354h2171f97637c02d6c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <97aa5afe0605231613l7fd8a354h2171f97637c02d6c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4473AEE4.2020108@subtend.net>

Robert McIntosh wrote:
> Hi,
> 
> I'd like to hear from some PLUGers about ISPs that provide leased static
> IPs
> for small home businesses.  Please provide rough price/month, service
> quality (customer and uptime), and any other comments.

Recently, I have experience with two providers, one local.

At home, I've been with EasyStreet since fall of 2004.  At the time I
pleaded with them to offer a product to match the Verizon 3Mb/1Mb
offering, which they did, and I think I was one of the first on this
package before it was finalized.

One static IP, downloads are fine, upstream is ~600-700Kb.  Support is
friendly, they will open the ticket with Verizon for you if there is an
outage, and every outage has been within Verizon's network.  Line+ISP
comes to ~$170/mo.

My company, OpenSourcery, has service provided by Comcast.  As a small
business and supporter of small business I have to hate Comcast, but
unfortunately they have exceeded every expectation (which in retrospect
was very low to begin with).  We have the small business service,
8Mb/1Mb, and a small block of static IP's.

A couple outages in the last 9 months.  Staff was better than expected
when I needed to set my reverse DNS for our static IP's.  Cost is ~$165/mo.

The downstream screams.  I constantly see 750-1100MByte/s downloads,
exceeding the advertised 8Mb/s down.  The upload is wimpy, and as we
depend on upstream more I may seek alternatives.

I'm hoping DSL will catch up to the cable company.  Wish I could find a
cheap DS3.  Unfortunately for EasyStreet and my ethics, FIOS is too
tempting if it arrives.  I also think asynchronous downstream/upstream
rates are a disservice to the Internet, but NANOGites and carriers would
disagree.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

On Tue, May 23, 2006 at 04:10:26PM -0700, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
 
> One of the cheaper ways to get a static IP address and oodles
> of bandwidth is an offsite virtual colocation server.

Even cheaper than a virtual colo account is a free DynDNS domain name
and a year's worth of outbound mail "smarthost" service ($15). All
your outgoing mail goes through their mail server (static IP
assignment and all that good stuff), and you can still receive
incoming mail on your own server (to your own domain).

On 5/23/06, Robert McIntosh <mcintoshrt at gmail.com> wrote:
> I'd like to hear from some PLUGers about ISPs that provide leased static IPs
> for small home businesses.  Please provide rough price/month, service
> quality (customer and uptime), and any other comments.

  I have Comcast business service, not because it was my first choice,
but because it was what I could get.

  The low end Comcast business service is $75 a month with a $250
setup fee.  I was impressed by the skill of the installers.  He was
*much* more skilled than the idiot dweeb they sent out to install my
cable television.  I also got my cable television service cleaned up
in the deal.  ;-)

  My Comcast service has been stable.  I've only had one small
problem.  There was a routing issue in their network that prevented an
IPSec tunnel originating on another network from setting up.  Comcast
fixed the problem quickly... after I had already troubleshot the
problem.  I told them which router... they fixed it...

  I *tried* to get Speakeasy DSL, but I was located too far from a
POP.  There was too much copper between my place and their DSLAM.
There *is* DLS in my area, but only from Qwest.  Qwest apparently does
not give Speakeasy access to the digital SLIC in my area.

  I had a very unpleasant time getting Speakeasy to remove erroneous
billing from my credit card.  I had to speak  with a supervisor and
make nasty threats before they would reimburse my modem and
installation fee.  Bastards!  It took several months for them to
remove the billing.

Qwest DSL, aka Microsoft Network, would not provide a fixed IP.

Steve D...
